Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Second Proof for Rational Good and Evil (Ḥusn wa Qubḥ)
Second rational proof for ḥusn wa qubḥ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for the rationality of good and evil and apply the concept of tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ to refute the opposing view.

Core Idea
If good and evil were not rational, God's preference would be arbitrary—an impossibility, so rationality is necessary.

This lesson examines the second rational proof used by Ḥanafī uṣūlīs to establish that good and evil are known rationally (ʿaqlī), not solely by revelation. The argument hinges on the impossibility of preference without a preferrer (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). We will follow the author’s refutation of an Ashʿarī objection and then walk through the logical structure of the proof, distinguishing the Ḥanafī position of general application (iṭlāq ʿāmm) from that of the Muʿtazila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof's claim and its required entailment
The proof claims: if good and evil were not rational, preference without a preferrer would be necessary. Understand why this is considered impossible in Islamic theology.
The impossibility of tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ is universally accepted.
2
Analyze the author's refutation of the objection about truth-falsity equivalence
The author rejects the Ashʿarī argument that truth and falsehood are equivalent in all respects, showing that their concomitants are contradictory, so equivalence is impossible.
This is a preliminary dialetic move before the main proof.
3
Trace the main proof: correlation (bayān mulāzama)
The proof proceeds by examining where the preferrer (murajjiḥ) can lie: in the act's essence, in the agent's essence, or in the agent's attribute. Each possibility is assessed, leading to the conclusion that the preferrer must be in the act itself.
4
Distinguish the Ḥanafī position from Muʿtazilī positions
The early Muʿtazila held the essence itself necessitates good/evil; the later Muʿtazila said an attribute in the essence does; the Ḥanafīs say good/evil are by iṭlāq ʿāmm (general application). Recognize the nuance.
Iṭlāq ʿāmm means the intellect perceives goodness/evil through general rational principles, not inherent essences.
5
Evaluate the thamara al-khilāf (practical consequence)
The dispute affects whether acts can be known as good or evil without revelation. The Ḥanafī view allows rational knowledge prior to revelation, while the Ashʿarī view denies it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب یہ ہے کہ مستدل نے دلیل مذکور میں مذکور استواء سے اگر یہ مراد لیا ہے کہ مقصود معین کی طرف نظر کرتے ہوئے فی الجملہ صدق و کذب میں استواء ہے تو یہ تسلیم ہے مگر اس سے حسن کا صدق کے لئے ذاتی ہونا لازم نہیں آتا کیونکہ اس میں امکان ہے کہ عقل نے صدق کو کذب پر ذاتی ہونے کی وجہ سے ترجیح نہ دیا ہو بلکہ کسی مرجع آخر کی وجہ سے ترجیح دیا ہو کم از کم اتنا امکان تو ہے ہی کہ بولنے والا اسی کا عادی ہے اور اگر استواء سے یہ مراد ہے کہ جمیع اغراض و مقاصد کی طرف نظر کرتے ہوئے صدق و کذب میں نفس الامر میں استواء ہے تو یہ تسلیم نہیں کیونکہ دونوں کے عوارض ولوازم کے ایک دوسرے کے منافی دمبائن ہونے کے باعث استواء محال ہے اور جب استواء محال ہے تو وقوع استواء کی تقدیر پر دوسرا استحالہ لازم آئیگا لہذا صدق کو بغیر مرج کے ترجیح دینا محال ہو جائے گا کہ محال مستلزم محال ہوتا ہے اور استحالہ کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ عدم ایثار نظر کرتے ہوئے استواء کی طرف محال نہیں ہے تو ایثار محال ہو گا ۔ دلیل (۲) : حسن و فتح کے عقلی ہونے پر بعض اصولین نے یہ دلیل دی ہے حسن و قبح عقلی ہے اگر عقلی نہ ہوں تو ترجیح بلا مرح لازم آئے گا اور ترجیح بلا مرجح باطل ہے اور جوستلزم بطلان ہو باطل لہذ احسن و فتح کا غیر عقلی ہونا باطل ہے اور عقلی ہونا ثابت ہو گیا۔
بیان ملازمت :۔ یہ ہے کہ اللہ عز شانہ نے بعض افعال جیسے زنا وشرب خمر وغیرہ کو حرام فرمایا ہے اور بعض افعال جیسے صوم و صلوۃ وغیرھما کو واجب قرار دیا ہے اسی طرح بعض جیسے کفر کو مناط عذاب کیا اور بعض جیسے ایمان کو مفاد ثواب قرار دیا ہے، پس ان مذکورہ افعال حسنہ وسینہ میں حسن و فتح اگر ذاتی نہ ہوں تو ترجیح بلا مرج لازم آئے گا وہ اسطرح کہ مریج یا تو ذات فعل ہی میں ہو گا حالانکہ اس جگہ اس کو مفقو دفرض کیا گیا ہے یا تو مرجح ذات فاعل میں ہوگا اور ذات فاعل میں مرج نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے کہ ذات فاعل اور اسکی صفت ارادہ کی نسبت کل فعل کی طرف برابر ہے اس لئے ان دونوں میں سے کوئی مرج نہیں ہو سکتے لہذا ضروری ہے کہ مرجح ذات فعل ہی میں ہو بایں طور پر کہ ذات فعل ہی مقتضی حسن وقیح ہے جیسا کہ متقدمین معتزلہ کا مذہب ہے، یا تو ذات فعل میں ایسی صفت ہے جو مقتضی حسن و قبیح ہے جیسا کہ متأخرین معتزلہ کا مسلک یا پھر حسن و قبح فعل میں اطلاق عام کے اعتبار سے ہے جیسا کہ حنفیہ کا مذہب ہے اور یہی مطلوب ہے۔
The point is that if the proponent, by the mentioned equivalence in the cited proof, intends that in view of a specific purpose, truth and falsehood are equivalent in their overall truth and falsity, then this is conceded. However, from this it does not necessitate that goodness is inherent to truth, because it is possible that the intellect did not prefer truth over falsehood due to its being intrinsic, but rather due to some other referent. At least it is possible that the speaker is accustomed to it. And if by equivalence he intends that in view of all purposes and objectives, truth and falsehood are equivalent in the matter itself, then this is not conceded, because their accidents and concomitants are mutually contradictory and opposite, making equivalence impossible. And when equivalence is impossible, then postulating its occurrence leads to another impossibility. Hence, preferring truth without a preferrer becomes impossible, since the impossible entails the impossible. The reason for impossibility is that if non-preference is not impossible in view of equivalence, then preference becomes impossible. Second proof: Some usuli scholars have given this proof for the rationality of good and evil: Good and evil are rational; if they were not rational, preference without a preferrer would be necessary, and preference without a preferrer is invalid. Whatever entails invalidity is invalid. Therefore, its not being rational is invalid, and its being rational is established. Explanation of the correlation: God the Exalted has prohibited some acts like adultery and wine-drinking and made others like fasting and prayer obligatory. Similarly, He made disbelief the basis of punishment and faith the basis of reward. Now, if in these mentioned good and evil acts, goodness and evil were not inherent, then preference without a preferrer would be necessary. That is because the preferrer must be either in the essence of the act (which is here assumed absent) or in the essence of the agent. But the essence of the agent cannot be a preferrer because the essence of the agent and its attribute of volition are equally related to all acts, so neither can serve as a preferrer. Hence, it is necessary that the preferrer be in the essence of the act itself, such that the act's essence itself necessitates goodness and evil (as the early Mu'tazila held), or that there is an attribute in the act's essence that necessitates goodness and evil (as the later Mu'tazila held), or that goodness and evil in the act are by virtue of general application (as the Hanafis hold). And this is what is intended.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument in the usul al-fiqh dispute over whether good (husn) and evil (qubh) are rational (aqli) or revealed (shar'i). The author first refutes an objection from the Ash'aris regarding the equivalence of truth and falsehood. He then gives a second proof: if good and evil were not rational, then God's assignment of reward and punishment would involve preference without a preferrer (tarjih bila murajjih), which is rationally impossible. The proof proceeds by analyzing where the preferrer lies: it cannot be in the agent's essence or will (since these are equal towards all acts), so it must be in the act itself. The Hanafi position is that good and evil are known by general application (itlaq 'amm), not by inherent nature. The passage also distinguishes the views of early Mu'tazila (essence itself), later Mu'tazila (an attribute in the essence), and Hanafis (general application). The argument is a classic example of rationalist theology in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Goodness and evil of actions; the moral valuation.
ترجیح بلا مرجح (tarjih bila murajjih): Preferring one thing over another without a preferrer—the absurdity that must be avoided.
اطلاق عام (itlaq 'amm): General application; the Hanafi position that goodness/evil are not intrinsic but known through general rational principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second proof in this passage, what would necessarily follow if good and evil were not rational?
A) Preference without a preferrer (tarjih bila murajjih)
B) That all acts would be equally good
C) That revelation would be unnecessary
D) That humans could not be held accountable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi proof for rational good and evil rests on the impossibility of arbitrary divine preference, establishing that reason can discern moral value through general principles.

The Hanafi Response to the Ash'ari Objection on Lying
Presents Ibn Arabi's proof and Ash'ari objection with Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between inherent evil and permissible necessity (hasan li-ghayrihi) as a response to the Ash'ari challenge.

Core Idea
Lying remains inherently evil but becomes permissible when it is the lesser of two evils.

This passage presents a critical debate in usul al-fiqh: if moral values are inherent, how can lying ever be acceptable? The Ash'aris argue that since lying is sometimes obligatory, inherent morality is false. The Hanafis respond by distinguishing between an act's inherent nature and its permissibility due to external reasons. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi position on moral objectivity and legal exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari objection
Read the matn: 'If it were inherent, it would not be absent, yet it is absent. For example, lying is obligatory to save a prophet or an innocent person.' The objection is that inherent morality cannot change, but lying does change status.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentator says: 'The answer is that here it is committing the lesser of two evils, not that lying has become good.' Note the key phrase: aqall al-qabihayn.
This response preserves the inherent evil of lying while allowing its permissibility.
3
Understand hasan li-ghayrihi
The concept that an act can be 'good by virtue of another' without being good in itself. The commentator says 'hasan li-ghayrihi does not negate inherent evil.'
This is crucial for distinguishing between inherent and acquired moral value.
4
Apply the maxim al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat
The commentator connects this to the well-known legal maxim. Necessities permit what is normally prohibited.
This maxim is used in many fiqh rulings, not just moral philosophy.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis maintain that the act is inherently evil but permissible; Ash'aris say the act's goodness is determined by revelation, not inherent nature.
This affects how one understands the relationship between reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل (۳): حسن و فتح کے عقلی ہونے پر درج ذیل دلیل کو حضرت شیخ محی الدین ابن عربی نے فتوحات مکیہ میں ارقام فرمایا ہے۔ دلیل کا خلاصہ خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ افعال کا حسن و فتح اگر شرعی ہو جیسا کہ اشاعرہ کہتے ہیں تو لازم آئے گا کہ بعثت رسول کے سبب سے بندہ کو شرائع کا مکلف بنانا بلاء ومصیبت میں مبتلا کرنا ہے، بعثت رحمت نہیں ہو سکتی اس لئے کہ درود شرع سے پیشتر لوگ آرام وسکون اور راحت و رفاهت اور رخصت میں تھے کہ استحقاق عقاب کا مطلقا تصور نہیں تھا لیکن درود شرع کے بعد شرع نے ان پر ایسی تکلیفات شاقہ انڈیل دی جس کی طبائع انسانی متحمل نہیں کیونکہ اگر وہ احکام شرعیہ کو ترک کرینگے تو عقوبات شدیدہ کے مستحق ہوتے ہیں پھر یہ معلوم ہے کہ طبائع انسانیہ تعیش پسند واقع ہوئے ہیں بلکہ اپنے نفسانی خواہشات کے اعتبار سے لذائذ محرمہ کی طرف مائل ہیں لیکن حکم ایجاب و تحریم ان پر سخت تنگی ہے لہذا شرع کی طرف سے ان پر احکام شدیدہ مسلط کرنا اور ترک و فعل پر ان کا استحقاق عقوبات ہونا، ان کو بلاء وفتنہ میں مبتلا کرنا ہے جب کہ وہ قبل بعثت رفاحت و راحت میں تھے اور یہ علیم و حکیم کی شان سے بعید ہے جس کی حکمت و قدرت جلی و وسیع ہے۔
متن المسلم : " وقالوا اولا لو كان ذاتيالم يتخلف وقد تخلف فان الكذب مثلاً يجب لعصمة بنى وانقاذ برى عن سفاك والجواب أن هناك ارتكاب اقل القبيحتين لا أن الكذب صار حسناً قيل يرد عليه ان هذا الكذب ههنا واجب فيدخل في الحسن اقول الحسن لغيره لاينافى القبح لذاته وهذا معنى قولهم الضرورات تبيح المحظورات غاية الامر انه يلزم القول بان كلامنهما كما انه بالذات كذالك بالغير ولعلهم يلتزمونه و به امكن لهم التخلص عن النسخ على انه لا يتم على الجبائية وعلينا
ترجمہ : اور اشاعرہ نے پہلی دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ اگر حسن و قبح ذاتی ہوتا تو کبھی فعل سے متخلف نہیں رہتا جب کہ کبھی فعل سے متخلف ہو جاتا ہے مثلا کسی نبی علیہ السلام کو ظالم سے اور محفوظ الدم کو سفاک سے بچانے کے لئے بیشک کذب واجب ہے اور جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں دو قبیحوں
Third proof: On the rationality of good and evil, Shaykh Muhyiddin Ibn Arabi has recorded the following proof in Al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya. The summary of the proof is that if good and evil of actions are revealed (shar'i) as the Ash'aris say, then it would follow that sending a messenger to burden the servant with obligations is subjecting him to trial and tribulation, not mercy. Because before the advent of revelation, people were in ease, tranquility, comfort, and concession, with no concept of deserving punishment at all. But after revelation, such burdensome obligations were imposed that human natures cannot bear. Because if they abandon the divine commands, they become liable to severe punishments. Moreover, human natures are pleasure-seeking, inclined towards forbidden pleasures based on their desires. The commands of obligation and prohibition are severely restrictive upon them. Therefore, imposing severe rules upon them and making them liable to punishment for omission or commission is subjecting them to trial and affliction, whereas they were in comfort before revelation. This is far from the station of the All-Knowing, All-Wise, whose wisdom and power are vast and extensive. Text of Muslim al-Thubut: "And they said firstly: If it were inherent, it would not be absent; yet it is sometimes absent. For example, lying is obligatory to protect a prophet or to save a free person from a murderer. The answer is that here it is committing the lesser of two evils, not that lying has become good. It was said in response: This lying here is obligatory, thus it enters into good. I say: Good by virtue of another does not negate inherent evil. This is the meaning of their statement: 'Necessities permit prohibitions.' The ultimate point is that it necessitates the statement that something is both inherently one thing and by virtue of another another thing. Perhaps they would concede it, and thereby they could escape the problem of abrogation. However, this does not hold for the Jabba'i and for us." Translation: The Ash'aris gave a first proof saying: If good and evil were inherent, they would never be absent from an act; yet they are absent sometimes. For example, lying is obligatory to save a prophet from an oppressor or to protect an innocent person from a murderer. The answer is that here it is committing the lesser of two evils, not that lying has become good. It was said in response: This lying here is obligatory, thus it enters into good. I say: Good by virtue of another does not negate inherent evil. This is the meaning of their statement: 'Necessities permit prohibitions.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents two important elements in the debate over rational good and evil. First, Ibn Arabi's proof argues that if good and evil were solely determined by revelation, then the sending of prophets would be a burden rather than a mercy, contradicting God's wisdom. Second, the matn from Muslim al-Thubut quotes the Ash'ari objection: if good/evil were inherent, then lying could never be good, yet it is permissible (even obligatory) in certain cases (e.g., to save a life). The Hanafi response is that lying in such cases is not intrinsically good; rather, it is a lesser of two evils. The commentator explains that 'good by virtue of another' (hasan li-ghayrihi) does not contradict inherent evil (qabih li-dhatihi). This is the principle that necessities permit prohibitions (al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat). The Ash'ari objection is thus refuted: the act remains inherently evil but becomes permissible due to a greater harm. The commentary also notes that this argument does not hold for the Jabba'i sect (a Mu'tazila subgroup) or for us (Hanafis? Actually 'upon us' might mean the Ash'aris themselves? The text is ambiguous). Overall, the Hanafi position avoids the extremes of both inherent morality and pure conventionalism.

Key Terms
حسن لغیره (hasan li-ghayrihi): Good by virtue of another; an act that is inherently evil but becomes permissible due to an external reason.
اقل القبیحتین (aqall al-qabihayn): The lesser of the two evils; a principle used to justify permissible actions in cases of conflict.
ضرورات تبیح المحظورات (al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat): Necessities permit prohibitions; a legal maxim allowing otherwise forbidden acts in dire need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Hanafi commentator respond to the Ash'ari claim that lying is sometimes obligatory and thus cannot be inherently evil?
A) He concedes that good/evil are not inherent.
B) He says lying becomes good in that context.
C) He says it is a case of committing the lesser of two evils.
D) He argues that the act is neither good nor evil.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position uses the concepts of hasan li-ghayrihi and aqall al-qabihayn to maintain that lying is inherently evil but can be permissible under necessity, refuting the Ash'ari objection.

The Ash'ari Argument Against Intrinsic Good and Evil: The Example of Lying
Response to Ash'ari proof using example of lying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Ash'ari argument against intrinsic good/evil using the example of lying and identify the Hanafi response based on choosing the lesser of two evils.

Core Idea
Lying remains evil even when obligatory; its permissibility is due to choosing the lesser harm, not a change in its nature.

One of the deepest debates in Usul al-Fiqh is whether actions are intrinsically good or evil, or whether these qualities are assigned by revelation. The Ash'aris argue that if lying were intrinsically evil, it could never become good, yet we find cases where lying becomes obligatory. In this passage, we examine the Ash'ari proof and the Hanafi response, which distinguishes between intrinsic nature and circumstantial permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opposing Argument
First, locate the Ash'ari claim: if good/evil were intrinsic, they would never separate from the act. They use lying as an example: lying is evil, but when saving a prophet it becomes obligatory, and obligatory is good, so lying becomes good. Therefore, good/evil are not intrinsic but purely from Shari'ah.
The Ash'ari conclusion: 'Thus it is known that good and evil are not intrinsic but Shari'.'
2
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi commentator rejects that lying becomes good. Lying remains evil. The doer is compelled to commit the lesser of two evils: lying vs. allowing harm to a prophet. The Prophet's hadith 'Choose the lesser of two evils' supports this. Lying is permissible not because it becomes good, but because it is the lesser evil.
The Hanafi position defends intrinsic evil of lying; the wajib nature does not change its essence.
3
Understand the Concept of 'Lesser Evil'
The principle of 'akhaff al-sharayn' (the lesser of two harms) operates when no purely good option exists. The action remains evil intrinsically, but its commission is required to avoid a greater evil. This is not a transformation of qubh into husn, but a practical necessity.
Analogy given: a person falling into fire may drown in water; drowning is not good, but it is the lesser hardship.
4
Apply to Analogous Cases
The same reasoning applies to other cases where a normally prohibited act becomes permissible due to necessity, e.g., breaking an oath to save a life. The act does not become intrinsically good; the ruling changes due to conflicting obligations.
This principle is foundational in Hanafi fiqh for resolving conflicts between haram and wajib.

قوله وقالوا اولا لو كان ذاتيا لم يتخلف الخ درج بالا عبارت میں حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر اشاعرہ کی دلیل کو ذکر کیا ہے۔ دلیل اول: ۔ اس دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ افعال کا حسن و قبح اگر ذاتی ہوتا جیسا کہ معتزلہ وغیرہ کا قول ہے تو ان دونوں میں سے کسی کو ذات فعل سے متخلف نہیں ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ جو شئی کی ذاتی ہوتی ہے یا ذات کا مقتضی ہوتا ہے وہ ذات سے منفک نہیں ہوتی بایں طور کہ فعل حسن کا حسن قبیح ہو جائے یا فعل قبیح کا قبح حسن سے بدل جائے ایسا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے جب کہ ہم دیکھتے ہیں کہ حسن فعل حسن سے اور قبح فعل قبیح سے کبھی منفک ہو جاتا ہے مثال کے طور پر کذب ایک فعل قبیح ہے بلکہ وہ افتخ القبائح ہے لیکن یہی کذب بعض اوقات میں حسن ہو جاتا ہے چنانچہ کسی نبی کی حفاظت و صیانت کے لئے یونہی کسی بری الدم کو ظالم سفاک سے بچانے کے لئے جھوٹ بولنا واجب ہے ایسے موقع پر جب ظالم کسی نبی کو مثلا قتل کرنے کے لئے تلاش کر رہا ہو یا محفوظ الدم کے خون بہانے پر آمادہ ہو لوگوں سے ان کا حال و پستہ پوچھ رہا ہو تو دیکھنے والے پر واجب ہے کہ نبی یا محفوظ الدم کا پتہ نہ بتائے بلکہ جھوٹ بول دے اور کہے مثلاً میں نے نہیں دیکھا ہے، مجھے ان کا علم نہیں یا یہ وہ شخص نہیں جس کو تم تلاش کر رہے ہو وغیرہ چونکہ اس پر کذب واجب ہے۔ اور اس میں کوئی خفاء نہیں کہ واجب حسن ہے، پس اگر کذب کا قبح ذاتی ہوتا تو کذب سے کبھی مختلف نہیں ہوتا کہ کذب حسن ہو جائے کیونکہ ذات کے مقتضی کا ذات سے متخلف ہونا ممتنع ہے اور صورت مذکورہ میں مقتضائے ذات ذات سے متخلف ہو گیا تو جان لیا گیا کہ حسن و قبح ذاتی نہیں ہے بلکہ شرعی ہے یہی ہمارا مطلوب ہے۔
قوله والجواب أن هناك ارتكاب الخ
اس عبارت سے ماتن نے اشاعرہ کی دلیل مذکور کا جواب دیا ہے۔
حاصل جواب :۔ یہ ہے ہم صورت مذکورہ میں یہ تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ ذاتی ، ذات سے یعنی قبح کذب سے متخلف ہو گیا ہے اور قبیح حسن ہو گیا یہاں تک کہ وہ لازم آئے جو وارد کیا گیا بلکہ کذب اپنی صفت قبح پر باقی ہے اس میں مطلقا حسن نہیں ہے، رہے فاعل کی مدح تو کذب کے حسن ہو جانے کی وجہ سے نہیں بلکہ اس صفت کی وجہ سے وہ قابل مدح ہے جو اس کو عارض و لاحق ہوئی وہ یہ ہے کہ فاعل دو قبیحوں میں سے ایک قبیح کے ارتکاب کرنے پر مجبور ہوایا تو نبی کی حفاظت وصیانت اور بری الدم کے بچانے کے لئے جھوٹ بولے یا پھر جھوٹ ترک کر کے سچ بولے اور نبی پر ظلم ہونے دے اور بری کو قتل ہونے دے۔ اور اس میں شک نہیں کہ اہلاک نبی اور قتل بری کے مقابل کذب اہون واقل ہے ،غرض کذب میں دو قباحتوں میں سے ایک اقل کا ارتکاب کرنا ہے نہ یہ کہ کذب حسن ہو گیا چنانچہ اسی طرف سید عالم ﷺ نے ارشاد فرمایا ” من ابتلی ببلیتین فلیختر اهونهما“ اسی وجہ سے فقہاء نے فرمایا کہ جو شخص آگ میں گر گیا اور اس کو آگ سے بچنے کے لئے کوئی چارہ کار نہیں سوائے اس کے کہ وہ دریا میں غرق ہو جائے تو اس کے لئے دریا میں غرق ہو جانا جائز ہے پس یہ جواز اس لئے نہیں ہے کہ اپنے نفس کا اہلاک حرام نہیں ہے بلکہ جواز اس لئے ہے کہ لامحالہ اس کو ہلاک ہونا ہی تھا آگ سے خواہ پانی سے، مگر غرق ہونے پر صبر کرنا آگ کی تپش پر صبر کرنے سے زیادہ آسان ہے۔
قوله-قيل يرد عليه أن الكذب ههنا واجب آخ جواب مذکور پر فاضل مرزا جان نے شرح مختصر کے حاشیہ میں ایک اشکال قائم کیا ہے اس عبارت میں ماتن نے اسی اشکال کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔
اشکال : ۔ یہ ہے کہ اس جگہ کذب واجب ہے جیسا کہ مجیب کو بھی اعتراف ہے اور ہر واجب چونکہ حسن ہے لہذا کذب حسن ہو گیا حالانکہ فاضل مجیب نے کہا کہ یہاں کذب حسن نہیں ہوا پھر جب حسن خصم کے نزدیک ذاتی ہے لہذا وہ کذب قبیح کے ساتھ جمع نہیں ہوسکتا تو کذب کو صدق پر ترجیح دینے کی وجہ صرف یہ ہے کہ وہ حسن ہو گیا نہ اس لئے کہ اقل امنین کا ارتکاب ہے۔
He said: They said firstly: If it were intrinsic, it would not be separate... The summary of this argument: If goodness and evil were intrinsic as the Mu'tazila claim, then neither would be separate from the action's essence... For we see that goodness sometimes separates from a good act and evil from an evil act. Example: Lying is an evil act, but sometimes becomes good, such as when protecting a prophet or an innocent person from a tyrant. In such a case, lying is obligatory, and what is obligatory is good. So if evil were intrinsic, lying could never become good... Therefore, goodness and evil are not intrinsic but Shar'i. This is our desired conclusion. He said: The answer is that there is commission... The summary of the answer: We do not accept that the intrinsic quality has separated. Lying remains on its quality of evil; it does not become good at all. The praise for the doer is due to an accidental quality: he is compelled to commit the lesser of two evils. So he lies to save the prophet or innocent, not because lying becomes good. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever is afflicted with two trials, let him choose the lesser of them.' Hence, a person falling into fire is allowed to drown in water to avoid the fire, not because self-destruction is permissible, but because it is the lesser hardship. He said: It was said: This is subject to the objection that here lying is obligatory, and every obligatory is good, so lying becomes good... The objection: Lying here is wajib, and every wajib is good, so lying becomes good. If goodness is intrinsic, it cannot coexist with evil, so the preference of lying over truth is only because it became good, not because it is the lesser evil.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate on whether the goodness (ḥusn) and evil (qubḥ) of actions are intrinsic (dhātī) or determined by Sharī‘ah (shar‘ī). The text presents the Ash‘arī argument using the example of lying (kadhib). Lying is normally evil, but in certain circumstances—like saving a prophet or an innocent person from a tyrant—it becomes obligatory. Since the obligatory is good, lying appears to become good. If lying were intrinsically evil, it could never become good. Therefore, the Ash‘arīs conclude that good and evil are not intrinsic but purely shar‘ī. The commentator (the mātn) responds: Lying does not actually become good; it remains evil. However, the doer is faced with two evils—allowing harm to a prophet/innocent or lying. He chooses the lesser evil, which is lying. This is a concession, not a change in intrinsic nature. The Prophet’s ḥadīth 'Choose the lesser of two evils' supports this. The commentator analogizes to a person falling into fire: he may drown in water because it is the lesser hardship. An objector then argues: if lying is wajib, it must be good; otherwise, how can you prefer it? The commentator answers that the goodness here is extrinsic (bi al-ghayr) due to necessity, not intrinsic. Thus, the Hanafī position defends the intrinsic nature of good and evil while acknowledging that Sharī‘ah can permit an evil act as a lesser harm.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): The quality of being good, either inherently (dhātī) or by legal designation (shar‘ī). In this passage, it describes an action deemed commendable.
قبح (qubḥ): The quality of being evil, either inherently (dhātī) or by legal designation. Here, lying is considered intrinsically evil.
ذاتی (dhātī): Intrinsic; a property that cannot be separated from the essence of an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the reason lying becomes permissible (wajib) when saving a prophet?
A) Lying becomes intrinsically good.
B) Lying becomes obligatory, and all obligatory acts are good.
C) Lying remains evil but is chosen as the lesser evil.
D) The nature of lying changes due to the intention.

Key Takeaway
Lying remains intrinsically evil even when obligatory; its permissibility is due to choosing the lesser harm, not a change in its nature.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Ash'ari's Second Proof on Husn and Qubh: The Contradiction Argument and Hanafi Response
Ash'ari rational argument and Hanafi critique on essential moral qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari logical argument against inherent moral qualities and state the Hanafi usul response distinguishing unconditional and conditional essentiality.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari proof fails because 'dhati' can mean conditional essentiality, avoiding contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine the second Ash'ari proof that moral goodness (husn) and evil (qubh) are determined by Shari'ah, not inherent in actions. The Ash'aris argue that if qualities were inherent, the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' would force a contradiction: truth (good) would necessitate lying (evil). The Hanafi response, rooted in a nuanced understanding of 'essentiality' (dhati), shows this proof only works under an unconditional definition. We will unpack the argument, trace the Hanafi critique, and see how this dispute reflects deeper usul principles about the nature of moral qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Find the sentence where the Ash'ari state their second proof. Note the structure: 'If it were essential, then two opposites would come together in the example of...' This is a conditional statement leading to a contradiction.
The key phrase in Arabic: 'لو كان ذاتياً لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل لاكذبن غدا'
2
Work through the logical implication
Understand how the example works: For the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' to be true, lying must actually occur tomorrow. Thus truth (good) necessitates lying (evil). The Ash'aris claim this shows that the same proposition involves both good and evil, an impossibility if goodness/evil are essential.
The argument is a qiyas (analogy) where the ruling of the necessary is transferred to the necessitator.
3
Locate the Hanafi response
The text states the proof is not complete against the Jabba'iyya and Hanafis. The Hanafi position is that husn/qubh are shara'i, not dhati. The response argues that 'essential' can have two meanings: unconditional (like four for four-ness) or conditional (like coldness of water, which can be overridden). The Mu'tazila, who claim essentiality, mean the second type, so no contradiction arises.
The discussion of two meanings of 'essential requisition' (iqtida dhati) is central.
4
Analyze the epistemological weight
Note that the Ash'ari argument is rational (dalil aqli) and is countered by a rational distinction. The thubut and dalala are both zanni, meaning the proof is not categorical. The author sides with the Hanafi/Ash'ari position that husn/qubh are shara'i.
The authority here is from the later Hanafi usul tradition, building on earlier discussions.
5
Synthesize the practical impact
The dispute determines whether actions like lying are evil before revelation. Hanafi/Ash'ari: no (they become evil only by revelation); Mu'tazila: yes (inherently evil). This affects how new cases without explicit texts are judged.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

نہیں ورنہ فتح لذاتہ کا واجب ہو نا لا زم آئے گا۔
۲۱۳
جواب : ۔ اشکال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بیت المقدس و بیت اللہ بذاتم حسن لذاتہ ہیں لیکن جس شریعت میں جس وقت بیت المقدس کی طرف توجہ کرنے کا حکم وجوبی تھا اس وقت توجہ الی بیت المقدس حسن تھا اس حسن کی وجہ سے جو توجہ کو ذات کے واسطہ سے واسطہ فی العروض کے بغیر ، عارض ہوا، پس جب اسوقت بیت المقدس کی طرف توجہ حسن ہے اگر چہ ذات کے واسطہ سے تو ظاہر ہے کہ کعبہ کی طرف توجہ قبیح بالعرض ہوگا کیونکہ اس وقت کعبہ کی طرف توجہ کرنا بیت المقدس کی طرف توجہ کے مانع ہے جس میں حسن بواسطہ الذات ہے، بعینہ یہی تقریر حسن توجہ الی الکعبہ میں جاری ہوگی کہ جب سید عالم صلى الله عليه وسلم کی شریعت غراء کا نزول ہوا اور کعبہ کی طرف توجہ کا حکم و جو بی صادر ہوا تو اس وقت بیت المقدس کی طرف توجہ کا حسن زائل ہو گیا اور یہ حسن توجہ الی الکعبہ کی طرف پلٹ گیا پس اسوقت جب کعبہ کی طرف توجہ کا حکم ہے تو اس صورت میں توجہ الی الکعبہ میں حسن ذاتی یا حسن عرضی پایا گیا اور توجہ الی بیت المقدس فتیح بالعرض ہوگیا کیونکہ اس وقت بیت المقدس کی طرف توجہ کرنا کعبہ کی طرف توجہ کے مانع ہے جس میں کہ حسن ہے۔ قوله - على انه لايتم على الجبائية وعلينا
حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر اشاعرہ نے جو دلیل دی ہے اس کی دلیل کا یہ دوسرا جواب ہے اس عبارت سے مصنف کہتے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کی دلیل مذکور جبائیہ اور ہم حنفیہ کے خلاف تام نہیں ہوتی ہے کیونکہ جبائیہ فعل کا حسن و قبح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں مانتے بلکہ صفت اعتباریہ کا قول کرتے ہیں اور ہم حنفیہ اطلاق عام کا قول کرتے ہیں البتہ یہ دلیل جمہور معتزلہ کے خلاف ضرور قائم ہوسکتی ہے جو حسن و قبح کے ذاتی ہونے کے قائل ہیں چونکہ یہ دلیل ذاتی ہونے کو منع کر رہی ہے اور رہے جبائیہ تو وہ اس کے قائل نہیں اور حنفیہ تو وہ حصر سے انکار کرتے ہیں۔ حق یہ ہے کہ یہ دلیل جمہور معتزلہ پر بھی تام نہیں ہوتی چنانچہ نا سبق میں گذر چکا ہے کہ اقتضاء ذاتی کا اطلاق دو معنوں پر ہوتا ہے ایک وہ مقتضی جس کی ذات بلا شرط زائد کے مقتضی ہو جیسے زوجیت اربعہ کے لئے پس ایسی ذاتی ( مقتضی ) کا ذات سے تخلف بالکل ممتنع ہے دوسرا وہ مقتضی کہ ذات اس کو من حیث ھی بھی چاہے جیسے برودت ماء کہ اس ذاتی کا ذات سے تخلف بعروض عارض جائز ہے چنانچہ برودت ما تسخن عارض ہونے کے وقت زائل ہو جاتی ہے حالانکہ برودت ذاتی ہے اور معتزلہ کی حسن و قبح کے ذاتی ہونے سے یہی معنی ثانی مراد ہے جو تخلف کے منافی نہیں ۔
متن المسلم : " وثانياً لو كان ذاتيا لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل لا كذبن غدا فان صدقه يستلزم الكذب وبالعكس وللملزوم حكم اللازم وربما يمنع ذالك الاترى أن المفضى الى الشر لا يكون شرا بالذات بل بالعرض قال الشيخ في الاشارات
الشر داخل فى القدر بالعرض اقول هذا يرشدك الى الالتزام المذكور سابقا فافهم :ترجمہ: اور اشاعرہ نے (حسن و قبح شرعی ہونے پر ) دوسری دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ اگر حسن و فتح ذاتی ہو تو لا کذ بن غدا جیسی مثال میں اجتماع نقیضین لازم آئے گا اس لئے کہ اس کا صدق کذب کو اور کذب صدق کو مستلزم ہے اور ملزوم کا حکم لازم کا حکم ہوتا ہے اور بسا اوقات اس پر منع دارد کیا جاتا ہے کیا تم نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ جو شر کی طرف مفضی ہو وہ شر بالذات نہیں ہوتا بلکہ شر بالعرض ہوتا ہے چنانچہ شیخ نے اشارات میں کہا کہ تقدیر میں شر بالعرض داخل ہے میں کہتا ہوں کہ یہ جواب اسی التزام کی طرف راہ نمائی کرتا ہے جو پہلے ذکر کیا جا چکا ہے اس لئے سمجھو!
تشريح - قوله وثانيا لوكان ذاتيا لاجتمع النقيضان الخ
اس عبارت سے ماتن نے حسن و قبیح کے شرعی ہونے پر اشاعرہ کی دوسری دلیل ذکر کی ہے۔ دلیل دوم: کا ماحصل یہ ہے کہ اگر افعال کا حسن و قبح ذاتی ہو تو لاکذ بن غدا والی مثال میں اجتماع نقیضین لازم آئے گا اور اجتماع نقیضین محال لہذا حسن و قبیح کا ذاتی ہونا محال ۔ اور اس مثال میں اجتماع نقیضین اس طرح لازم آئے گا کہ اس کلام (لاکذ بن غدا) کا صدق کذب کو ستلزم ہے بایں طور کہ صدق، کلام کا واقع کے مطابق ہونے کا نام ہے اور یہ کلام لاکذ بن غدا اس وقت واقع کے مطابق ہوگا جب اس کلام کے قائل سے آنے والے کل میں کذب کا تحقق ہو پھر جب اس کلام کے قائل سے آئندہ کل کذب کا صدور ہوا تو وہ کلام لانذ بن غداً صادق ہو گیا، الغرض اس کلام کے صدق کے لیے کذب قبیح 11 زم ہے اور جب صدق کے لئے کذب لازم تو ظاہر ہے کہ کذب فتیح کے لئے صدق ملزوم ہو گا اور یہ مسلم ہے کہ جو ملزوم یا ستلزم قبیح ہے قبیح ہوتا ہے تو لازم آئے گا کہ شئی واحد مثلا صدق کے لئے حسن و قبیح
(Page 186) ... No, otherwise the opening of the essence would necessitate obligation... Answer: The answer to the objection is that Bayt al-Maqdis and Bayt Allah are good in themselves, but in whichever shari'ah and at whichever time the command to face Bayt al-Maqdis was obligatory, at that time facing Bayt al-Maqdis was good due to that goodness which accrued to the act of facing through the means of the essence without any intermediary. So when at that time facing Bayt al-Maqdis is good, even if through the essence, then it is clear that facing the Ka'bah is evil by accident because at that time facing the Ka'bah prevents facing Bayt al-Maqdis which has goodness through the essence. Exactly this same reasoning will apply to the goodness of facing the Ka'bah when the noble Shari'ah of the Prophet (PBUH) was revealed and the obligatory command to face the Ka'bah was issued, then at that time the goodness of facing Bayt al-Maqdis ceased and this goodness transferred to facing the Ka'bah. So at that time when the command is to face the Ka'bah, in this case facing the Ka'bah has essential or accidental goodness, and facing Bayt al-Maqdis became evil by accident because at that time facing Bayt al-Maqdis prevents facing the Ka'bah which has goodness. His statement: 'Rather it is not established against the Jabba'iyyah and against us.' This is the second response to the proof that the Ash'aris gave for husn and qubh being Shari'ah-based. The author says that the aforementioned proof of the Ash'aris is not complete against the Jabba'iyyah and us Hanafis. Because the Jabba'iyyah do not believe that an action's husn and qubh are due to the essence of the action; rather they hold it to be an attribute of consideration. And we Hanafis hold the general application. However, this proof can certainly be established against the majority of the Mu'tazila who believe in the essential nature of husn and qubh, since this proof denies essentiality. As for the Jabba'iyyah, they do not believe in it, and the Hanafis deny the exclusivity. The truth is that this proof is not even complete against the majority of the Mu'tazila, as has passed earlier in the preface that the application of essential requisition (iqta' dhati) has two meanings: one is the requisite whose essence necessitates without any additional condition, like the quadrilaterality for four-ness, so such an essential requisite cannot ever be absent from the essence; the second is the requisite that the essence desires in itself, like the coldness of water, where the absence of this essential from the essence is permissible due to the occurrence of an accident. Thus coldness ceases when heating occurs, even though coldness is essential. And what the Mu'tazila mean by essential husn and qubh is this second meaning, which is not inconsistent with absence. (Page 187) The matn of Musallam: 'Secondly, if it were essential, then the two opposites would come together in an example like 'I will not lie tomorrow,' for its truth necessitates lying, and vice versa. And the thing that necessitates has the ruling of the necessary. And sometimes this is prevented. Do you not see that what leads to evil is not evil in essence but by accident? The Sheikh said in al-Isharat: 'Evil enters into destiny by accident.' I say: This guides you to the commitment mentioned previously, so understand.' Commentary: The author mentions the second proof of the Ash'aris for husn and qubh being Shari'ah-based. Summary of the second proof: If husn and qubh of actions were essential, then in the example of 'I will not lie tomorrow' it would necessitate the conjunction of two opposites. The conjunction of two opposites is impossible, so essential husn and qubh are impossible. In this example, the conjunction of opposites would occur as follows: the truthfulness of this statement necessitates lying, because truthfulness is conformity of speech to reality, and this statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' conforms to reality only when lying actually occurs from the speaker tomorrow. Then when lying occurs from the speaker tomorrow, the statement becomes true. Thus, for the truth of this statement, lying (which is evil) is necessary. And if truth necessitates lying, then truth is the thing that necessitates, and it is accepted that whatever necessitates what is evil is itself evil. So it would necessitate that one thing, e.g., truth, has both goodness and evil.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on whether moral goodness (husn) and evil (qubh) are inherent in actions (dhati) or determined by divine command. It presents the second Ash'ari proof against inherent moral qualities and the Hanafi response. The Ash'aris argue that if actions were inherently good or evil, then a statement like 'I will not lie tomorrow' would force a contradiction: for the statement to be true, lying must occur, making truth (good) necessitate evil, thus one act would be both good and evil. However, the commentator (matn author) and the sharh explain that this reasoning is flawed because the Mu'tazila mean by 'essential' a conditional essentiality that can be overridden by accidents. The Hanafi position is that husn and qubh are shara'i (determined by revelation), and the Ash'ari argument fails to refute either the Jabba'iyyah (who deny essentiality entirely) or the Hanafis (who reject the exclusivity). The truth, as established earlier, is that essential requisition can be understood in two ways: one absolute and unconditional, another which can be prevented by external factors. The Mu'tazila intend the second meaning, so no impossibility arises. This lesson demonstrates the rational interplay between Ash'ari and Hanafi usul principles.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Moral goodness or beauty of an act, according to this text it is determined by Shari'ah (not inherent).
قبح (qubh): Moral evil or ugliness of an act, determined by Shari'ah.
ذاتي (dhati): Inherent, essential; in this context, moral quality that belongs to an act in itself regardless of external factors.
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidental; a quality that obtains due to external circumstances, not the essence.
اجتماع النقيضين (ijtima' al-naqidayn): Conjunction of two opposites; a logical impossibility.
لاكذبن غدا (la kadhuban ghadan): 'I will not lie tomorrow'; a future-tense proposition used in the Ash'ari argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Ash'ari argument using 'I will not lie tomorrow', what contradiction is claimed?
A) The statement is both true and false simultaneously.
B) Truth (good) necessitates lying (evil), so the same act would be both good and evil.
C) The act is neither good nor evil.
D) The statement is impossible to evaluate.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof against inherent moral qualities fails because it assumes unconditional essentiality, whereas the Hanafi/Mu'tazili understanding of 'essential' is conditional and can be overridden by revelation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Ash'ari Second Proof and Its Refutation
Ash'ari proof and its critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari second proof against intrinsic moral values and evaluate the Hanafi-oriented critique of that proof.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari argument that intrinsic goodness/badness leads to logical contradictions is rebutted by denying that the necessitator's ruling is intrinsically required.

The Ash'ari school of theology argued that moral goodness (husn) and badness (qubh) are not inherent in actions but are determined by divine command. One of their key proofs uses a logical paradox involving the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow.' This lesson unpacks that argument and then presents the objection raised by later scholars, including those within the Hanafi usul tradition, who challenge a crucial premise. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate deductive arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari Proof
State the claim: if goodness/badness were intrinsic, then a statement like 'I will not lie tomorrow' would force a contradiction. Understand the example: the truth of the statement requires the speaker to actually lie tomorrow, making truth (good) necessitate a lie (bad). Similarly, the falsehood of the statement requires the speaker not to lie, making a lie (bad) necessitate truth (good).
Focus on the logical relation: truth  lie mutual necessity.
2
Analyse the Contradiction
If truth necessitates lie, and lie necessitates truth, then the same act (e.g., truth-telling) would be both good (because it is truth) and bad (because it necessitates a lie). Since two opposites cannot coexist, the assumption that goodness/badness are intrinsic must be false.
This is the core of the proof: reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the Objection (Man')
Scholars like Mirza Jan, Abhari, and Qadi 'Adud object to the premise that 'the ruling of the malzum (necessitator) is the same as the ruling of the lazim (necessary thing).' They argue that the transfer of moral quality may be accidental (bi-'arad), not intrinsic. For example, the mission of prophets is intrinsically good but leads to destruction of disbelievers, which is bad accidentally.
The objection denies the necessary intrinsic transfer of value.
4
Evaluate the Impact
If the objection holds, the Ash'ari proof does not disprove intrinsic moral values; it only shows that the logical relation is not necessarily one of intrinsic value. This opens the door for the Mu'tazila and others to maintain that actions have moral qualities in themselves, though reason may need revelation to know them fully.
The Hanafi position typically acknowledges rational discernment but holds that ultimate legal rulings depend on Shari'ah.

The text of al-Muslim: 'And secondly, if it were intrinsic, then two opposites would meet in examples like 'I will not lie tomorrow' because its truthfulness necessitates lying and vice versa, and the ruling of the necessary thing applies to the thing that necessitates it. Sometimes this is objected: don't you see that what leads to evil is not evil in itself but accidentally? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: evil enters into divine decree accidentally. I say: this guides you to the commitment mentioned earlier, so understand.' Translation: The Ash'aris, when proving that goodness and badness are Shar'i, gave a second proof: if goodness and badness were intrinsic, then in the example of 'I will not lie tomorrow', a contradiction would follow, because the truth of this statement necessitates lying, and lying necessitates truth, and the ruling of the thing that necessitates is the ruling of the necessary. Sometimes this is objected: don't you see that what leads to evil is not evil in itself but accidentally? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat that evil enters into divine decree accidentally. I say: this guides you to the commitment mentioned earlier. Commentary: The author [al-Muslim] mentions the second proof of the Ash'aris that goodness and badness are Shar'i. The substance of the second proof: If the goodness and badness of actions were intrinsic, then in the example of 'I will not lie tomorrow', a contradiction would be necessary, and contradiction is impossible, so intrinsic goodness/badness is impossible. The contradiction arises as follows: the truth of this statement necessitates lying because truthfulness is correspondence with reality, and this statement corresponds to reality only if the speaker actually lies tomorrow. If the speaker lies tomorrow, then the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' becomes true. So for its truth, a bad lie is necessary. And since a bad lie requires truth as its necessitator, and it is accepted that the thing that necessitates badness is bad, it follows that one and the same thing (e.g., truth) would be both good and bad, or good and not good, which is a contradiction. Similarly, the lie of this statement necessitates truth: lie is non-correspondence with reality, and this statement does not correspond if no lie occurs tomorrow. So for its lie, non-occurrence of lie (i.e., truth) is necessary. Thus the lie (bad) necessitates truth (good), so truth would be both good and bad. This is impossible, so intrinsic goodness/badness is false; rather, truth despite being good would be bad, and lie despite being bad would be good. The author's saying 'sometimes this is objected' refers to the objection made by Fadil Mirza Jan, Abhari in the margin of Sharh Mukhtasar, and Qadi 'Adud in al-Mawaqif. The gist of the objection: The Ash'ari claim that the ruling of the necessitator is the same as the ruling of the necessary, in a real intrinsic sense, is not conceded, because it is not necessary that the necessitator of good is intrinsically good, or the necessitator of bad is intrinsically bad. Rather, the correct view is that the necessitator of good is good accidentally, and similarly the necessitator of bad is bad accidentally. These scholars present evidence: don't you see that what leads to evil is not evil in itself, but what leads to evil is in itself good? For example, the coming of great messengers is intrinsically good, but it also causes the destruction of many disbelievers. If what leads to evil were necessarily evil, then the mission of prophets would be evil despite being mercy and wisdom, which no rational Muslim would dare to say. They support this objection with the statement of Shaykh Abu Ali Sina in al-Isharat that evil enters into divine decree secondarily and accidentally, while divine decree primarily and intrinsically concerns good, but because the existence of much good depends on the existence of little evil, and because of little evil... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of the Ash'aris (theologians) that moral goodness (husn) and badness (qubh) are not intrinsic qualities of actions but are determined by divine law (Shar'). The proof is based on a logical argument: if goodness/badness were intrinsic (dhati), then a statement like 'I will not lie tomorrow' (la akdhibanna ghadan) would entail a contradiction. If the speaker tells the truth, the statement is true only if he lies the next day, meaning the truth (good) necessitates a lie (bad). Conversely, if the statement is false, it requires that he does not lie, so the lie (bad) necessitates truth (good). Thus the same action (either truth or lie) would be both good and bad, which is impossible. Therefore, goodness/badness cannot be intrinsic; they must be conferred by the Lawgiver. The commentator then notes that some scholars (Mirza Jan, Abhari, Qadi 'Adud) object to this proof. They deny the premise that the ruling of the necessitating thing (malzum) is necessarily the same as the ruling of the necessary thing (lazim) in an intrinsic sense. Instead, the necessitator may be good or bad accidentally ('arad). For example, the mission of prophets is intrinsically good but leads to the destruction of disbelievers, which is bad accidentally. This objection is supported by Ibn Sina's statement in al-Isharat that evil enters divine decree accidentally, not intrinsically. Thus, the Ash'ari proof is refuted by showing that the relation of necessitation does not transfer intrinsic moral value.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Goodness or moral praiseworthiness of an action
قبح (qubh): Badness or moral blameworthiness of an action
ذاتي (dhati): Intrinsic, inherent in the essence of the thing
بالعرض (bil-'arad): Accidentally, not as a primary attribute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Ash'ari second proof, why does the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' present a problem for intrinsic moral values?
A) Because if the statement is true, then the speaker did not lie, making truth good.
B) Because if the statement is false, then the speaker lied, making lying bad.
C) Because truth and lie would both be necessitated by each other, making the same action both good and bad.
D) Because the statement implies that lying is sometimes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof fails to demonstrate that intrinsic moral values are impossible, because the transfer of value from necessitator to necessary may be accidental rather than intrinsic.

The Hanafi Rebuttal to Ash'ari on Inherent Good and Evil
Refuting the Ash'ari claim via premise denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi objection to the Ash'ari claim that good and evil are not inherent, by identifying the contested premise and presenting evidence for accidental moral valuation.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not inherent in actions; they are accidental, determined by Shariah, not by pure reason.

In this passage, an Ash'ari argument is presented: if good/evil were inherent, a lie that necessitates truth would be both good and evil – a contradiction. Therefore, good/evil are not inherent. Hanafi scholars respond by denying a key premise: that the ruling of the antecedent (malzum) necessarily transfers to its consequent (lazim). They argue that something leading to evil is not essentially evil but accidentally evil, as seen in prophetic missions and jihad. This lesson unpacks the logical structure, the Hanafi rebuttal, and the broader usul principle at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari argument and its logical premise
The Ash'ari argue: if falsehood (evil) necessitates truth (good), then falsehood would be both good and evil – a contradiction. Hence, good/evil cannot be inherent. Their key premise is that the ruling of the consequent (lazim) transfers to the antecedent (malzum).
2
Locate the Hanafi denial of that premise
Hanafi scholars (Mirza Jan, Abhari, Qadi 'Adud) deny that the ruling transfers essentially. They claim that the antecedent of good/evil is only accidentally good/evil, not essentially.
3
Analyze the examples given (prophetic missions, jihad)
Prophets coming is essentially good but leads to destruction of disbelievers (evil); jihad is obligatory despite causing death. This shows that what leads to evil is not inherently evil.
4
Understand the concept of accidental (bil-'arad) evil
Evil can be accidental: coldness that spoils fruits is not evil in itself but relative to fruits. Similarly, oppression and adultery are evil only from certain perspectives.
5
Synthesize the practical import for Usul al-Fiqh
For Hanafis, moral valuation is not intrinsic to actions; it is determined by Shariah. This affects how we interpret divine commands and derive rulings.

مفضی الی الشر بالذات شر نہیں ہوتا اسکی مزید توضیح محقق طوسی نے شرح اشارات میں کیا ہے کہ مثلاً برودت جو شمار ( پھلوں) کو فاسد کر دیتی ہے وہ فی نفسہ شرنہیں نہ اس حیثیت سے کہ برودت ایک کیفیت ہے اور نہ ہی اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ اس کی علت موجبہ ہے ہاں البتہ برودت شمار کی طرف قیاس کرتے ہوئے شر ہے کہ وہ شمار کے امزجہ کو فاسد کر دیتی ہے۔ بلکہ زنا ظلم اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ دونوں دو قوت غصبیہ وقوت شہو یہ سے صادر ہوتے ہیں شر نہیں ہیں بلکہ اس حیثیت سے تو ان دونوں سے دونوں قوتوں کے کمال کا اظہار ہوتا ہے ہاں ظلم وز نا دونوں شر ہیں نظر کرتے ہوئے مظلوم کی طرف، سیاست مدینہ کی طرف اور اس نفس ناطقہ کی طرف جو دونوں قوت حیوانیہ کی ضبط کے متحمل نہیں ہیں۔ الغرض شر بالذات ان اشیاء میں سے کسی ایک کے کمال کا فقدان ہے رہے اس کے اسباب پر جو شر کا اطلاق کیا جاتا ہے وہ شر بالعرض ہے کہ وہ ان اشیاء کی ہلاکت کی طرف مودی ہوتا ہے انتھی“ اس تقریر سے معلوم ہو گیا کہ مفضی الی الشر بالذات شر نہیں ہوتا بلکہ شر بالعرض ہوتا ہے ... اسی طرح مفضی الی الخیر کا بالذات اس حیثیت سے خیر ہونا بھی ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ خیر بالعرض ہوتا ہے نہیں بلکہ مفضی الی الخیر بھی کبھی شر ہو سکتا ہے۔

فائدہ:۔ احتمالات عقلیہ کے اعتبار سے خیر وشر کی کل پانچ قسمیں ہیں ۔
(۱) خیر محض ۔ (۲) شرمحض ۔ (۳) خیر وشر مساوی ۔ (۴) خیر غالب شرمغلوب۔ (۵) شر غالب خیر مغلوب۔
(۱) خیر محض وہ ہے جو عدم کو نہ قبول کرے اس لئے کہ عدم شر محض ہوتا ہے یہ قسم واجب تعالیٰ کے ساتھ خاص ہے۔
or good and not good both exist, and this is a contradiction. Similarly, the preceding statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' necessitates truth from falsehood. For falsehood is a statement not corresponding to reality, and this statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' will correspond to reality only if no falsehood occurs from the speaker on the coming day. So if indeed no falsehood occurs from the speaker of this speech tomorrow, then this statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' will be a lie. In short, for this speech to be false, the non-occurrence of falsehood, i.e., truth, is necessary. And when truth is necessary for falsehood, then clearly falsehood is evil (qabih) and that is its necessary consequent, and it is established that whatever necessitates good is good, because the ruling of the consequent is the ruling of the antecedent. So it would follow that a single thing, for example, falsehood, would be both ugly and good, or both ugly and not ugly, which is a contradiction, and a contradiction is false. Therefore, the essential (dhati) nature of good and evil is false. Rather, truth, despite being good, would be evil, and falsehood, despite being ugly, would become good. His saying: 'perhaps one may deny that... do you not see...'

The aforementioned proof of one premise has been denied by the eminent Mirza Jan and Abhari in the gloss on Sharh al-Mukhtasar, and by Qadi 'Adud in al-Mawaqif. The above expression refers to this denial. The denial: The summary of the denial is that we do not accept the Ash'ari claim that the ruling of the antecedent is essentially the ruling of the consequent, because it is not necessary that the antecedent of good is essentially good, nor that the antecedent of evil is essentially evil. Rather, the correct view is that the antecedent of good, and similarly the antecedent of evil, are good and evil accidentally (bil-'arad) and secondarily (bil-'arad). The eminent Mirza Jan and like-minded scholars, while presenting their denial, say: Do you not see that that which leads to evil is not essentially evil, but rather that which leads to evil is essentially good, like the coming of the great messengers is essentially good, yet it is also a cause of destruction for many disbelievers and polytheists. If it were necessary that whatever leads to evil is itself evil, then it would follow that the mission of the prophets, despite being mercy and wisdom, would be evil, which no rational Muslim can dare to say. While presenting the authority of the Master for their denial, they say that indeed Shaykh Abu Ali Sina (Avicenna) said in al-Isharat: 'Evil enters divine decree accidentally and secondarily, while divine decree pertains primarily and essentially to good. But since the existence of abundant good is contingent upon the existence of a small evil, and abandoning abundant good due to a small evil is unbecoming of the Wise, He decreed evil and granted it existence. For example, consider jihad: Allah Almighty has made jihad obligatory upon the Prophet and his Muslim community, despite the fact that it involves the destruction of disbelievers and polytheists and their ruin, but there is abundant good attached to it far outweighing the destruction of disbelievers, such as the exaltation of Allah's word, the implementation of these divine commands which contain goodness in this world and the Hereafter, the illumination of truth and the lamp of faith, and the extinguishing of the darkness of disbelief and transgression, etc.'

That which leads to evil is not essentially evil. A further clarification by al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi in Sharh al-Isharat: 'For example, coldness that spoils fruits is not evil in itself, neither inasmuch as coldness is a quality, nor inasmuch as it is the causative cause. Rather, coldness is evil only when measured against fruits because it spoils the temperaments of fruits. Even oppression and adultery, inasmuch as they emanate from the irascible and concupiscent powers, are not evil; in that respect they manifest the perfection of both powers. However, oppression and adultery are evil only when viewed from the perspective of the oppressed, the governance of the city, and the rational soul that cannot control these animal powers. In short, essential evil is the lack of perfection in any of these things. As for the causes to which evil is attributed, that is accidental evil because it leads to the destruction of those things.' End quote. From this exposition it becomes clear that what leads to evil is not essentially evil but accidentally evil. Similarly, what leads to good is not essentially good in that respect; rather it is accidentally good. No, rather what leads to good can sometimes be evil.

Benefit: According to rational possibilities, there are five types of good and evil:
1. Pure good.
2. Pure evil.
3. Equal good and evil.
4. Predominant good with subdued evil.
5. Predominant evil with subdued good.
(1) Pure good: that which does not admit non-existence, because non-existence is pure evil. This type is specific to the Necessary Existent (Allah).
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary from Ashraf al-Nu'ut on a section of Musallam al-Thubut, engaging in a theological-legal argument about the ontological nature of good (husn) and evil (qubh). The author first presents an Ash'ari argument attempting to prove that good and evil cannot be inherent (dhati) in actions, because that would lead to logical contradiction. For example, the statement 'I will not lie tomorrow' is considered a lie if no lie occurs; the lie is evil but necessitates truth (good), so the same thing would be both good and evil—impossible. Hence, they argue, good and evil are not intrinsic but divinely determined. The author then records a rebuttal by distinguished Hanafi commentators (Mirza Jan, Abhari, and Qadi 'Adud) who deny a key premise: that the ruling of the antecedent is essentially the ruling of the consequent. They assert that something leading to evil (mufdi ila al-sharr) is not inherently evil; rather, it may be good accidentally. They cite the mission of prophets, jihad, and a philosophical discussion by Avicenna and Tusi to show that evil often enters accidentally. The passage concludes with a classification of five types of good and evil based on rational probability, noting that pure good is unique to Allah. This discussion is part of the broader Hanafi-Maturidi position that good and evil are known through revelation, not through innate reason, and that divine commands are not subject to inherent moral valuation.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Goodness; in this passage, the quality of an action that merits praise reward.
قبح (qubh): Evilness; opposite of husn.
بالذات (bi al-dhat): Essentially, by virtue of its own essence.
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally, by virtue of external relation.
ملزوم (malzum): The antecedent (cause) of a necessary consequence.
لازم (lazim): The necessary consequence.
مفضی الی الشر (mufdi ila al-sharr): That which leads to evil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars cited, how is the 'leading to evil' (mufdi ila al-sharr) characterized?
A) It is essentially evil.
B) It is accidentally evil or good.
C) It is always good.
D) It is neither good nor evil.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school denies that moral qualities transfer from a necessary consequence to its antecedent; actions are not inherently good or evil but are evaluated by Shariah.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Goodness and Badness: Intrinsic and Accidental
Defense of Hanafi position on husn and qubh as contingent on consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between husn/qubh bi al-dhat and bi al-'arad to the example of the command 'do not lie about tomorrow' and evaluate the Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
Goodness and badness are both intrinsic and accidental; the ruling follows the necessary consequence, not the act itself.

This passage defends the Hanafi position that actions can be morally evaluated both inherently and by their consequences. The author uses the example of a command prohibiting lying about tomorrow to show how truthfulness can become evil if it leads to lying, and lying can become good if it leads to truth. This nuanced view is central to Hanafi usul, especially in allowing istihsan based on consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing position
State the Ash'ari claim that any obligatory act must be good in itself (because Shari'ah only commands good).
2
Clarify the Hanafi distinction
Explain the Hanafi view: actions have intrinsic moral qualities (husn/qubh bi al-dhat) but rulings can also attach accidentally (bi al-'arad) based on necessary consequences.
3
Work through the example
Analyze the command 'la takdhib 'an ghadin'. On the assumption that lying does not occur, truthfulness is good in itself but becomes bad because it leads to violating the command. On the assumption that lying occurs, lying is bad in itself but becomes good because it leads to truthfulness (obeying the command).
The ruling is for the necessary consequent, not the act.
4
Identify the contradiction in Mirza Jan's objection
Mirza Jan claimed that since lying becomes obligatory in this case, it must be good. But he previously accepted the Hanafi commitment. The author shows this is self-contradictory because obligation here is conditional and goodness is accidental.
The objection is refuted by the Hanafi framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله - اقول هذا يرشدك الى الالتزام الخ اس عبار سے ماتن اشاعرہ کی دلیل اول پر فاضل مرزا جان کے اعتراض پر ذکر کردہ جواب کی اہمیت وافادیت بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کی دوسری دلیل کا فاضل صاحب نے جو جواب دیا ہے اس سے ظاہر ہے کہ ہمارا التزام مذکور صحیح قابل تسلیم ہے چنانچہ ہم نے وہاں کہا تھا کہ حسن و قبیح جس طرح بالذات ہوتے ہیں بالعرض بھی ہوتے ہیں پس حکم لازم ملزوم کے لئے بالذات نہیں بالعرض ہے لہذا لا کذ بن غداً والی مثال میں بر بنائے عدم صدور کذب، صدق حسن بالذات ہے اور قبیح ہے اپنے لازم کذب کے واسطہ سے اور بر بنائے صدور کذب، کذب فتیح بالذات ہے اور حسن ہے اپنے لازم صدق کے واسطہ سے لہذا خوب دھیان سے سمجھو کہ اس میں کوئی پوشید گی نہیں ہے مگر دقیق ہے۔ قوله فافهم - مصنف نے فانھم سے فاضل مرزا جان پر تعریض کیا ہے کہ ہمارے التزام مذکور کو تسلیم کرنے کے بعد اشاعرہ کے استدلال اول کے جواب پر فاضل صاحب کا یہ اعتراض کرنا کہ یہ کذب واجب ہے اور ہر واجب حسن ہوتا ہے لہذا کذب حسن ہے قطعاً غیر مناسب اور اپنے قول کو اپنے قول سے دفع کرنا ہے۔ اس نکتہ فانھم کو خوب ذہن نشین کر لو اس لئے کہ اس راز سر بستہ اور نکت طیف سے تمام شرحوں کا دامن خالی ہے۔ متن المسلم : " وثالثا أن فعل العبد اضطرارى فان الممكن مالم يترجح لم
His statement: 'I say, this guides you to commitment, etc.' This expression explains the importance and benefit of the aforementioned response to the objection of Fadil Mirza Jan on the first proof of the Ash'aris. The answer that the Fadil gave to the second proof of the Ash'aris shows that our aforementioned commitment is correct and acceptable. Therefore we said there: goodness and badness, just as they are in essence, are also by accident. Thus, the ruling is for the necessary consequent not in itself but by accident. Hence, in the example of 'Do not lie about tomorrow,' on the basis of the non-occurrence of lying, truthfulness is good in itself and bad by virtue of its necessary consequence of lying; and on the basis of the occurrence of lying, lying is ugly in itself and good by virtue of its necessary consequence of truthfulness. Therefore, understand well that there is no concealment in it, but it is subtle. His statement: 'So understand' — the author, by 'so understand them (fahum),' makes an allusion to Fadil Mirza Jan: after accepting our aforementioned commitment, his objection to the response to the first proof of the Ash'aris — that this lying is obligatory and every obligatory thing is good, so lying is good — is utterly inappropriate and amounts to refuting his own statement. Understand this point well, for all commentaries are devoid of this hidden secret and subtle point. The text of al-Muslim: 'And thirdly, that the action of the servant is compelled; for the contingent, unless it is preponderated...'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the Ash'ari proofs regarding good and evil. The author refers to a previous discussion where Fadil Mirza Jan raised an objection to the first proof of the Ash'aris. The response to the second Ash'ari proof is now presented to affirm the Hanafi position that goodness and badness can be both intrinsic (bi al-dhat) and accidental (bi al-'arad). The key example is the command 'Do not lie about tomorrow' (la takdhib 'an ghadin). If we assume that lying does not occur, then truthfulness is good in itself but becomes evil because it necessarily leads to lying (since the command prohibits lying, if truthfulness results in breaking the command, it becomes evil). Conversely, if lying occurs, lying is inherently ugly but becomes good because it necessarily leads to truthfulness (by obeying the command not to lie, paradoxically lying becomes the means to truth). This shows that the ruling (hukm) attaches to the necessary consequence, not to the act itself. The author then rebukes Mirza Jan for objecting that since lying is obligatory in this case (to prevent a greater evil), it must be good; the author argues that this contradicts his own acceptance of the Hanafi commitment. The passage ends by quoting the matn of Muslim al-Thubut beginning the third proof that human actions are compelled (idhtirari), which will be discussed next.

Key Terms
حسن بالذات (husn bi al-dhat): Goodness in itself, inherent moral value.
قبیح بالذات (qubh bi al-dhat): Ugliness in itself, inherent moral badness.
کذب (kadhib): Lying, a speech act that contradicts reality.
صدق (sidq): Truthfulness, speech that corresponds to reality.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory, a legal category distinct from fard in Hanafi fiqh.
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally, by virtue of something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Do not lie about tomorrow,' on the basis of the occurrence of lying (i.e., if lying occurs), what is the moral status of lying according to the passage?
A) It is good in itself and becomes good by consequence.
B) It is ugly in itself but becomes good by consequence.
C) It is ugly in itself and remains ugly.
D) It becomes obligatory and therefore good in itself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an action can be inherently bad yet become good (and even obligatory) due to its necessary beneficial consequences, a principle that underpins istihsan.

Ash'ari's Third Proof for Shar'i Husn and Qubh and Hanafi Response
Ash'ari proof from contingency and Hanafi refutation via wajib bil ikhtiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third Ash'ari proof for the shar'i nature of husn and qubh, and explain the Hanafi refutation using the distinction between wajib bil ikhtiyar and idtirar.

Core Idea
Necessity by choice is not compulsion — the voluntary act remains freely chosen and rationally evaluable.

This passage presents the third Ash'ari argument for the position that good and bad are only known through revelation, not reason. The argument claims human actions are compulsory because they are contingent and require a preponderant. The Hanafi commentator refutes this by distinguishing between necessity by choice and compulsion, and by pointing to the voluntary nature of divine actions.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari position and the third proof
Summarize the Ash'ari claim that human actions are compulsory (idtirari) and therefore cannot be rationally evaluated as good or bad.
The proof is based on the logic of contingency: a contingent thing needs a preponderant to exist.
2
Analyze the key premises: contingency, preponderance, necessity
Break down the syllogism: (1) Human action is contingent; (2) A contingent cannot exist without a preponderant; (3) Once preponderated, existence becomes necessary; (4) Therefore the action is compulsory.
The conclusion hinges on whether 'necessary' implies 'compulsory'.
3
Present the Hanafi response: distinction between wajib bil ikhtiyar and idtirar
The Hanafi commentator argues that something can be necessary by choice (wajib bil ikhtiyar) without being compelled. The obvious difference between a voluntary movement and a tremor demonstrates this.
The response also invokes divine actions: Allah's acts are necessary yet not compulsory.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi refutation
Consider the strength of the refutation: Does the analogy with voluntary movement hold? Does the divine example refute the Ash'ari argument completely?
This step develops critical thinking about the dispute.
5
Conclude with the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For Hanafis, reason can independently determine that acts like prayer are good; for Ash'aris, their goodness depends wholly on revelation.
This shows the impact of the dispute on fiqh methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : " وثالثا أن فعل العبد اضطرارى فان الممكن مالم يترجح لم يوجد وترجيح المرجوح محال فمالم يجب لم يوجد فلايكون حسناً ولا قبيحا عقلا اجماعاً وهذا احسن واخصر ممافى المختصر والجواب أن الواجب بالاختيار لايوجب الاضطرار ضرورة الفرق بين حركتي الاختيار و الرعشة على انه منقوض بفعل البارى تعالى

ترجمه : اور اشاعرہ نے حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر تیسری دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ بندہ کا فعل اضطراری ہے کیونکہ ممکن کا جانب وجود جب تک راج نہ ہو موجود نہیں ہوسکتا اور مرجوح کی ترجیح محال ہے لہذا ممکن جب تک واجب نہ ہوگا موجود نہ ہوگا اس لئے عقلا و اجماعاً بندہ کا فعل حسن و قبیح نہیں ہوسکتا ۔ یہ دلیل مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کے بہ نسبت زیادہ عمدہ اور مختصر ہے۔ اور جواب یہ ہے کہ واجب بالاختیار اضطرار کو لازم نہیں کرتا کیونکہ حرکت اختیار یہ اور حرکت رعشہ میں بدیہی فرق ہے علاوہ ازیں یہ دلیل فعل باری تعالیٰ سے منقوص ہے۔

تشریح: -قوله - وثالثاً أن فعل العبد اضطرارى الخ درج بالا عبارت میں مائن نے حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر اشاعرہ کی تیسری دلیل ذکر کی ہے۔ دلیل سوم : ۔ کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ فعل عبد ممکن ہے اور چونکہ ممکن کا وجود و عدم مساوی ہوتا ہے لہذا جب تک اس کے جانب وجود کو کسی مرجح سے ترجیح نہ ہو خواہ وہ ترجیح حد وجوب کو پہونچتی ہو خواہ نہ پہونچتی ہو اسکا وجود نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ ممکن کے وجود و عدم کے مساوی ہونے کے سبب اسکا موجود ہونا محال وممتنع ہے پھر جب ممکن کے جانب وجود کو کسی مرجح سے ترجیح حاصل ہوگئی تو اسکا جانب وجود راج اور اسکا جانب عدم مرجوع ہو گیا اور یہ اپنی جگہ ثابت کہ مرجوع (ممکن کا جانب عدم ) کی ترجیح اور اسکا تحقق عتقلا شرعاً اور اجماعاً محال ہے۔ اور جب عدم محال ہوا تو ظاہر کہ عدم کی نقیض یعنی جانب وجود، واجب ہوگا ، لہذا ثابت ہو گیا کہ ممکن کا جانب وجود جیتک واجب نہ ہوگا ممکن موجود نہیں ہوسکتا۔ اور جب بندہ کے فعل کا جانب وجود واجب ہوا تو بندہ سے اس فعل کا صدور لازم ہو گیا اس حیثیت سے کہ اس کا ترک ممکن نہیں، نتیجتا بندہ اپنے فعل میں مضطر، مجبور اور غیر مختار ہو گیا کہ فعل واجب ہے اور فاعل مضطر کا فعل عقلا واجماعاً حسن و قبیح نہیں ہوتا چنانچہ عقلاء میں موافق، مخالف ہر ایک کا اتفاق ہے کہ فعل اضطراری
Text of al-Muslim: 'And thirdly, that the action of the servant is compulsory; for as long as the contingent (mumkin) is not preponderated it does not exist, and preponderating the preponderated (marjuh) is impossible; so as long as it is not necessary (wajib) it does not exist, therefore it is not good nor bad rationally by consensus; and this is better and more concise than what is in al-Mukhtasar. The answer: that what is necessary by choice (wajib bil ikhtiyar) does not necessitate compulsion, due to the necessary difference between the voluntary movement and the movement of tremor; moreover it is refuted by the action of Allah Most High. Translation: And the Ash'aris, in presenting the third proof for good and bad being shar'i, said that the action of the servant is compulsory, because the side of existence of a contingent cannot exist until it is preponderant, and preponderating the preponderated is impossible; therefore as long as the contingent is not necessary, it will not exist; hence rationally and by consensus the servant's action cannot be good or bad. This proof is finer and more concise than the proof mentioned in al-Mukhtasar. The answer is that wajib bil ikhtiyar does not necessitate compulsion, because there is an obvious difference between voluntary movement and the movement of tremor; besides, this proof is refuted by the action of Allah Most High. Commentary: -His saying: 'and thirdly that the action of the servant is compulsory' etc. In the above expression, al-Muslim (the author of the matn) has mentioned the third proof of the Ash'aris for good and bad being shar'i. Summary of the third proof: The action of the servant is contingent, and since the existence and non-existence of a contingent are equal, as long as its side of existence is not given preponderance by a preponderant (murajjih), whether that preponderance reaches the limit of necessity or not, it cannot exist, because due to the equality of existence and non-existence of the contingent, its existence is impossible and prevented. Then when the side of existence of the contingent gains preponderance from a preponderant, its side of existence becomes preponderant (rajih) and its side of non-existence becomes preponderated (marjuh). And it is established in its place that the preponderance of the preponderated (i.e., the side of non-existence of the contingent) and its actualization is rationally, legally, and by consensus impossible. And when non-existence becomes impossible, then obviously the opposite of non-existence, i.e., the side of existence, becomes necessary (wajib). Hence it is established that as long as the side of existence of the contingent is not necessary, the contingent cannot exist. And when the side of existence of the servant's action becomes necessary, then the occurrence of that action from the servant becomes necessary, in the sense that its omission is not possible. Consequently, the servant becomes compelled, forced, and non-choosing in his action, because the action is necessary, and the action of a compelled doer is rationally and by consensus not good or bad. Indeed, among the rational, both agreeing and opposing, there is agreement that a compulsory action is...'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari school's third argument for their position that good and bad (husn and qubh) are determined solely by divine command, not by rational judgment. The argument proceeds from the nature of contingent existence: any contingent being (mumkin) requires an external preponderant (murajjih) to tip the scales from non-existence to existence. Until that preponderance reaches the level of necessity (wujub), the contingent cannot exist. Therefore, the servant's action becomes necessary once the preponderant acts, and thus the servant is compelled (mudtar) — he has no real choice. A compelled act cannot be rationally evaluated as good or bad; such evaluations only apply to voluntary acts. Consequently, the Ash'aris conclude that husn and qubh are exclusively shar'i (revealed) categories. The Hanafi commentator (presumably the author of Ashraf al-Nu'ut) gives two refutations. First, the key distinction: something can be 'wajib bil ikhtiyar' — necessary by virtue of choice, not by external compulsion. The obvious difference between a voluntary movement (like raising one's hand) and the involuntary tremor of a palsy sufferer shows that necessity does not imply compulsion. Second, the argument is refuted by analogy with Allah's actions: Allah acts by necessity of His essence (wajib al-wujud), yet He is not compelled; His actions are perfectly voluntary. Thus the proof is invalid. The commentary (tashrih) then elaborates on the reasoning step-by-step, confirming that the Hanafi position — that reason can independently discern good and bad — remains intact.

Key Terms
اضطرارى (idtirari): Compulsory or necessitated action without genuine choice
واجب بالاختيار (wajib bil ikhtiyar): That which is necessary due to free choice, not external compulsion
حسن وقبح (husn wa qubh): Good and bad as moral-legal categories applicable to actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the key refutation of the Ash'ari claim that human actions are compulsory?
A) Human actions are not contingent but necessary.
B) The wajib bil ikhtiyar does not imply compulsion, as seen in voluntary vs. involuntary movement.
C) The Ash'ari proof contradicts the Quran.
D) Human actions are good or bad only by revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that human actions are voluntary and rationally evaluable, refuting the Ash'ari contingency proof by distinguishing between necessity by choice and compulsion.
