Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Ash'ari Argument for Shari'i Good and Evil
Presents the Ash'ari third proof in summary form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Ash'ari third proof for Shari'i good and evil and identify its logical structure.

Core Idea
A contingent action, once preponderated, becomes necessary and thus involuntary—so no rational good or evil.

The Ash'ari school holds that moral good and evil are determined exclusively by revelation, not reason. One of their key proofs argues that human actions are involuntary because a contingent (mumkin) requires a preponderant to tip the scales, making its existence necessary. This lesson unpacks that proof and sets the stage for understanding the Hanafi refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The Ash'aris claim that the servant's action is involuntary, hence it cannot be rationally evaluated as good or evil. Recognize the conclusion they aim to prove.
This is the target of the proof.
2
Trace the contingency premise
The servant's action is a contingent entity (mumkin): its existence and non-existence are equally possible. Notice that a contingent cannot exist without a preponderant (murajjih).
This is the starting point of the logical chain.
3
Understand the preponderance step
Once a preponderant tips the balance toward existence, the side of existence becomes preponderant and the side of non-existence becomes preponderated. Preference of the preponderated (tarjih al-marjuh) is impossible rationally and by consensus.
Thus non-existence becomes impossible, so existence becomes necessary.
4
Derive the necessity of the action
Since non-existence is impossible, existence is wajib (necessary). Consequently, the action necessarily occurs from the servant; he cannot omit it. The servant is therefore compelled (mudtarr) and not voluntary.
This is the key move: contingent → preponderance → necessity → compulsion.
5
Conclude the denial of rational good and evil
An involuntary action cannot be subject to moral evaluation (husn/qubh) rationally, because reward and punishment presuppose choice. The Ash'aris cite consensus on this point.
Thus only Shar'i law determines good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمه : اور اشاعرہ نے حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر تیسری دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا کہ بندہ کا فعل اضطراری ہے کیونکہ ممکن کا جانب وجود جب تک راج نہ ہو موجود نہیں ہوسکتا اور مرجوح کی ترجیح محال ہے لہذا ممکن جب تک واجب نہ ہوگا موجود نہ ہوگا اس لئے عقلا و اجماعاً بندہ کا فعل حسن و قبیح نہیں ہوسکتا ۔ یہ دلیل مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کے بہ نسبت زیادہ عمدہ اور مختصر ہے۔ اور جواب یہ ہے کہ واجب بالاختیار اضطرار کو لازم نہیں کرتا کیونکہ حرکت اختیار یہ اور حرکت رعشہ میں بدیہی فرق ہے علاوہ ازیں یہ دلیل فعل باری تعالیٰ سے منقوص ہے۔

قوله وهذا احسن واخصر ممافى المختصر اس عبارت سے ماتن نے دلیل مذکور کی عمدگی اور جامعیت و اختصار کو بیان کیا ہے کہتے ہیں کہ یہ دلیل ثالث جو شرح مختصر میں مرزا جان کے حاشیہ سے ماخوذ ہے مختصر ابن حاجب میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کے بہ نسبت بڑی عمدہ اور نہایت ہی مختصر دلیل ہے ، چنانچہ ماتن نے اس دلیل کی عمدگی اور اس کے کمال اختصار پر گفتگو کرتے ہوئے اور مختصر ابن صاحب کی دلیل پر نقص و طوالت کا ذکر کرتے ہوئے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے ۔
قال في المختصر واستدل فعل العبد غير مختار فلايكون حسنا ولا قبيحاً لذاته اجماعاً لانه اذا كان واجباً فى نسخة (لازما) فواضح وان كان جائزاً فان افتقر الى مرجح عاد التقسيم والا فهو اتفاقى وتقرير ذالک فی شرح العضدی ولا يخفى عليك انه يرد عليه انه مبنى على نفى الاولوية وربما يمنع كونه اتفاقياً لوجود السبب وفيه مافيه انتهت
مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کی توضیح و تقریر شرح عضدی میں مندرجہ ذیل الفاظ کے ساتھ مذکور ہے جن کو فاضل خیر آبادی وغیرہ شراح مسلم نے ذکر کیا ہے۔ وهو أن الفعل ان كان لازم الصدور عن العبد بحيث لا يمكنه الترك فظاهر انه غير مختار بل فعله اضطرارى وان كان جائزا وجوده وعدمه فان افتقرالی مرجح فمع المرجع يعود التقسيم فيه بان يقال ان كان لازماً فاضطراري والا احتاج الى مرجح آخر ولزم التسلسل وان لم يفتقر الى مرجح بل يصدر عنه تارة ولا يصدر أخرى مع تساوى الحالتين من غير تجدد امر من الفاعل فهو اتفاقي انتهى. یعنی بندہ کا فعل غیر مختار ہے لہذا وہ لذاتہ حسن و قبیح نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ فعل عبد اس تقدیر پر یا تو واجب ہوگا کہ بندہ سے لازماً اسکا صدور ہو گا اس حیثیت سے کہ بندہ کو اس کا ترک ممکن نہیں تب تو ظاہر ہے کہ بندہ غیر مختار ہے اور اس کا فعل اضطراری ہے یا یہ ہے کہ فعل عبد کا وجود عدم دونوں جائز ہوگا
Exists, and preference of the preponderated is impossible. So unless it becomes obligatory, it does not exist. Therefore it is not good or evil rationally, by consensus. And this is better and shorter than what is in al-Mukhtasar. The answer is that the obligatory by choice does not necessitate compulsion, due to the necessary difference between voluntary movement and tremor; moreover it is refuted by the act of Allah Almighty. Translation: The Ash'aris, in their third proof for good and evil being Shari'i, said that the servant's action is involuntary because the side of existence of a contingent cannot exist unless it becomes preponderant, and preference of the preponderated is impossible. Therefore a contingent cannot exist until it becomes obligatory. So rationally and by consensus, the servant's action cannot be good or evil. This proof is better and shorter than the proof mentioned in al-Mukhtasar. The answer is that the obligatory by choice does not necessitate compulsion, because there is an evident difference between voluntary movement and tremor; moreover this proof is refuted by the act of Allah Almighty. Explanation: His saying: 'And thirdly that the servant's action is involuntary' etc. In the above passage, the author mentioned the Ash'aris' third proof for good and evil being Shari'i. The summary of the third proof is that the servant's action is contingent, and since the existence and non-existence of a contingent are equal, unless its side of existence is given preponderance by a preponderant, whether that preponderance reaches the limit of obligation or not, it cannot exist because the contingent's existence and non-existence being equal makes its existence impossible. Then when the side of existence is given preponderance by a preponderant, its side of existence becomes preponderant and its side of non-existence becomes preponderated. And it is established in its place that preference of the preponderated (the side of non-existence of the contingent) and its actualization are impossible rationally, legally, and by consensus. When non-existence becomes impossible, obviously its opposite, i.e., the side of existence, becomes obligatory. So it is established that the side of existence of a contingent cannot exist until it becomes obligatory. When the side of existence of the servant's action becomes obligatory, then the occurrence of that action from the servant becomes necessary in such a way that its omission is not possible. Consequently, the servant in his action becomes compelled, coerced, and non-voluntary, because the action is obligatory and the action of one who is compelled is not good or evil rationally and by consensus. Thus there is agreement among rational people, both agreeing and opposing, that an involuntary action is not fit for reward or punishment. His saying: 'And this is better and shorter than what is in al-Mukhtasar.' With this phrase, the author expresses the excellence, comprehensiveness, and brevity of the mentioned proof. He says that this third proof, taken from Mirza Jan's gloss on Sharh al-Mukhtasar, is much better and very brief compared to the proof mentioned in Mukhtasar Ibn Hajib. So the author, while discussing the excellence and utmost brevity of this proof and mentioning the deficiency and length of Ibn Hajib's proof, said in the gloss: He said in al-Mukhtasar: 'And they argued: The servant's action is not voluntary, so it is not good or evil in itself by consensus, because if it is obligatory (in a version: necessary), it is obvious; if it is permissible, then if it needs a preponderant, the division returns; otherwise it is accidental.' And its elucidation is in Sharh al-Adudi. And it is not hidden from you that it is subject to the objection that it is based on the negation of priority, and perhaps its being accidental is prevented due to the existence of a cause, and it has its flaws. The explanation of the proof mentioned in al-Mukhtasar is found in Sharh al-Adudi with the following words, mentioned by Fadil Khayrabadi and other commentators of Muslim. 'It is that if the action is necessarily occurring from the servant such that he cannot omit it, then it is obvious that he is not voluntary, rather his action is involuntary. If its existence and non-existence are permissible, then if it needs a preponderant, with the preponderant the division returns: if it is necessary, then it is involuntary; otherwise it needs another preponderant and leads to regress. If it does not need a preponderant but rather occurs sometimes and not other times with equal states without any new factor from the agent, then it is accidental.' That is, the servant's action is not voluntary, so it cannot be good or evil in itself. Because the servant's action is either such that it necessarily occurs from him without possibility of omission, then it is obvious that the servant is non-voluntary and his action is involuntary; or the existence and non-existence of the servant's action are both permissible.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is part of a Hanafi commentary on the matn 'Muslim al-Thubut' (by Muhbb Allah al-Bihari), likely authored by 'Ashraf al-Nu'ut' (a gloss by Ashraf al-Din?). The passage addresses the Ash'ari argument that human actions are involuntary, hence cannot be rationally assessed as good or evil. The Ash'ari proof relies on the principle that a contingent (mumkin) requires a preponderant (murajjih) for its existence, which makes its existence necessary (wajib) once preponderated. Thus the servant's action is necessary, not voluntary, and thus not subject to rational evaluation. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, the necessary by choice does not necessitate compulsion, as seen in the difference between voluntary movement and an involuntary tremor. Second, the argument is refuted by the act of Allah, who acts voluntarily but without external compulsion. The commentator then expands on the proof from al-Mukhtasar and its critique, showing that the Ash'ari proof leads to infinite regress or accidental occurrence, both of which fail to negate voluntary action. The lesson thus defends the Hanafi position that human actions are voluntary and therefore can be morally evaluated both rationally and legally.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Moral goodness, understood rationally or legally.
قبح (qubh): Moral evil, counterpart of husn.
اضطرار (idtirar): Compulsion; acting without choice.
واجب بالاختيار (wajib bil-ikhtiyar): Obligatory by choice; an act that becomes necessary due to one's free selection.
مرجح (murajjih): Preponderant; factor that tips the balance of a contingent toward existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the Ash'ari third proof as presented in the passage?
A) Human actions are contingent and therefore can be good or evil rationally.
B) Human actions are involuntary, so they cannot be good or evil rationally.
C) Human actions are voluntary but only revelation defines good and evil.
D) Human actions are necessary but still subject to rational evaluation.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari claim that human actions are involuntary relies on the premise that a contingent action becomes necessary upon preponderance, but this necessity does not negate the voluntary nature of the act—a distinction the Hanafis exploit in their refutation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi Refutation: Wajib bil-Ikhtiyar and the Voluntary Act
Presents the Hanafi refutation of the Ash'ari third proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi refutation of the Ash'ari proof by distinguishing wajib bil-ikhtiyar from idtirar and applying it to the act of Allah.

Core Idea
Obligatory by choice (wajib bil-ikhtiyar) does not necessitate compulsion—voluntary and involuntary movements are fundamentally different.

Having presented the Ash'ari proof, the commentator now gives the Hanafi response. The refutation focuses on a logical distinction: an action can be necessary (wajib) yet remain voluntary (ikhtiyari). The example of Allah's own acts—necessary yet fully voluntary—serves as a decisive counterexample. This lesson unpacks that refutation and explores its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refutation's core claim
The Hanafi response states: 'The obligatory by choice does not necessitate compulsion.' This is the key premise. Recognize that the Ash'ari proof mistakenly infers compulsion from necessity.
The distinction is between ontological necessity and psychological compulsion.
2
Examine the voluntary vs. involuntary distinction
The text gives an intuitive example: voluntary movement (like raising one's hand) is fundamentally different from an involuntary tremor (ra'sha). This difference is known necessarily (bidariha) and shows that choice can coexist with a determined outcome.
If the Ash'ari proof were true, all actions would be like tremors, which is false.
3
Apply the refutation to Allah's actions
The Ash'ari proof is also refuted by the act of Allah Almighty. Allah's acts are necessary (not contingent), yet He acts voluntarily, not under compulsion. This shows that necessity does not entail compulsion.
If the proof held, it would also apply to Allah, leading to absurdity.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute affects whether human actions can be morally evaluated before revelation. The Hanafi position (supported by this refutation) allows rational moral evaluation, which is the basis for practices like istihsan and the recognition of maqasid al-shari'a.
This is why the Hanafi school can uphold rational good and evil.

Key Terms

اضطرار (idtirar): Compulsion; acting without choice.
رعشة (ra'sha): Tremor; involuntary bodily movement.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Wujub Bil-Ikhtiyar Does Not Imply Compulsion
Refuting proofs for compulsion in human actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wujub bil-ikhtiyar and wujub bil-idhtirar, and explain why the Hanafi view preserves human agency.

Core Idea
Obligation from choice does not negate choice — it confirms it, unlike involuntary necessity.

This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut defends the Hanafi position that human actions are not compelled even when they are necessary (wajib). The author refutes the Ash'ari argument that any necessary action must be compulsory, and shows that actions arising from one's own choice remain voluntary. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to the Hanafi usul al-fiqh approach to human responsibility and legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari objection
Identify the Ash'ari claim: if an action is wajib (necessary to occur), it must be idhtirari (compulsory), negating the agent's choice.
This is based on the idea that necessity and choice are incompatible.
2
Grasp the Hanafi distinction
Recognize the two types of wujub: wujub bil-ikhtiyar (obligation through choice) and wujub bil-idhtirar (obligation without choice, like a tremor).
The key is the presence or absence of the agent's will and intention.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that wujub bil-ikhtiyar does not necessitate idhtirar; instead, it reinforces choice. The intuitive difference between a voluntary movement and a tremor is self-evident.
Both movements are wajib in the sense of occurring, but the former is by choice, the latter is not.
4
Evaluate the practical consequence
If all wajib actions were idhtirari, there would be no foundation for praise, blame, reward, or punishment. The Hanafi position preserves moral accountability.
This thamarah al-khilaf is crucial for Islamic theology of actions.

تو وہ کسی مرج کا محتاج ہے یا نہیں اگر مرج کا محتاج ہے تو تقسیم مرجح کے ساتھ پلٹے گی بایں طور کہ یہ فعل مع المريح لازم و واجب ہے یا جائز ، بر تقدیر اول اضطراری ہوگا اور بر تقدیر ثانی مریج آخر کا محتاج ہوگا ، پھر اس مرجع آخر پر کلام جاری ہوگا کہ واجب ہے یا جائز ، اگر واجب ہے تو اضطراری اور جائز ہے تو مرجح ثالث کا محتاج ہوگا ، اس طرح تسلسل لازم آئے گا اور اگر مرج کا محتاج نہیں ہے کہ فعل عبد کا صدور کبھی ہوتا ہے اور کبھی نہیں ہوتا ہے اس کی دونوں حالت، فاعل کی طرف سے کسی تجدد کے بغیر، برابر ہے تو وہ اتفاقی ہے۔ قوله في الحاشيه ولا يخفى عليك انه يردانه مبنى على نفى الاولوية مختصر کی دلیل پر اعتراض کرتے ہوئے فاضل مرزا جان نے شرح مختصر کے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے " انه مبنی على نفى الاولوية اعتراض : کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل فعل عبد کے اضطراری ہونے پر کافی نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ دلیل نفی اولیت پر موقوف ہے یعنی اس دلیل میں صرف بندہ کے فعل وجوبی اور اتفاقی کو بیان کیا گیا ہے اور فعل اولیٰ جو جواز سے بالا اور وجوب سے کمتر ہوتا ہے کونہیں بیان کیا ہے اس لئے ایسا ممکن ہے کہ بندہ کا فعل مرجح کے ساتھ اسکا صدور اولی ہو اور عدم صدور جائز ہو تو اس صورت میں بندہ سے فعل کا صدور بغیر وجوب واتفاق کے ہوگا اور بندہ مضطر بھی نہ ہوالہذ ادلیل مختصر مذکور کا اضطرار پر تمامیت کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ اولیت کی بھی نفی کی جائے جبکہ دلیل مختصر سے اس کی نفی نہیں ہورہی ہے۔ نیز فعل ممکن کو اتفاقی کہنا منع ہے اس لئے کہ امکان علت مرفقہ کا محتاج ہوتا ہے اور جب علت مرجمہ کے سبب اس فعل کا وجود ہوا تو اتفاقی نہیں ہوسکتا یا جو علت مرجمہ کا محتاج ہو وہ اتفاقی نہیں ہوتا۔
اس کے برخلاف مصنف نے جو طرز استدلال اختیار کیا ہے وہ استدلال نفی اولیت پر موقوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ راج جبکہ اولی غیر واجب ہو تو اولی کا وقوع جائز ہے اور اولی کے ہوتے ہوئے غیر اولی کا وقوع محال ہے کہ وہ مرجوح کی ترجیح ہے اور مرجوح کی ترجیح محال۔ پس اس اعتبار سے مصنف کی دلیل مختصر کی دلیل سے احسن ہے نیز الفاظ وعبارات کے اعتبار سے مختصر کی دلیل طویل ہے جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے علاوہ ازیں اتفاقی کا قول زائد غیر مناسب ہے اس لحاظ سے مسلم میں ذکر کردہ دلیل مختصر سے بھی مختصر ہے۔ قوله في الحاشيه فيه مافيه مختصر کی دلیل کے ایک جز واتفاقی ہونے پر اعتراض کیا گیا تھا کہ فعل اتفاقی ممکن ہے اور ممکن بغیر علت و سبب کے نہیں پایا جاتا ہے اس لئے وجود سبب و علت کے وقت فعل اتفاقی نہیں ہو سکتا۔ مصنف نے اپنے قول فیہ مافیہ سے اس منع مذکور کے جواب کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔ جواب : یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ فعل اتفاقی کو سبب مرج کے ساتھ فرض کیا جائے تا ہم جب وہ لازم الصدور نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اسکا وجود و عدم دونوں برابر ہوتا ہے ( جائز ہوتا ہے ) ہاں اس سبب مرجح کے سبب اس کے وجود کو تر جیح رہتی ہے اور کسی دوسرے مرجع کا بھی محتاج نہیں رہتا ہے تو ضرور اسکا اتفاقی ہونا لازم آئے گا کیونکہ اتفاقی سے وہ مراد ہے کہ مرح نام کے بغیر جو موجود ہو جائے پس اس وقت فاعل ضرور غیر مختار ہوگا کہ اتفاقی غیر مختار ہوتا ہے چونکہ فاعل سے اس کے قصد وارادہ کے بغیر صادر ہوتا ہے حاصل یہ ہے کہ یہاں اتفاقی سے یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ جو اصلاً بغیر سبب کے ہو یہاں تک کہ منع وارد کیا جائے بلکہ اتفاقی سے مراد یہ ہے کہ جو فاعل کے قصد وارادہ سے نہ ہو گر چہ وہ سبب وعلت سے صادر ہو پس اس معنی کی تقدیر پر کوئی شک نہیں رہ جاتا کہ اتفاقی مدح وذم اور ثواب و عقاب کا مستحق نہیں رہ جاتا۔
قوله والجواب أن الوجوب بالاختيار لايوجب الخ اس عبارت سے ماتن نے اشاعرہ کی دلیل ثالث کا جواب دیا ہے۔ جواب : - کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ فعل عبد کے وجوب کے سبب سے فعل کا اضطراری ہونا اور بندہ کا منظر ہو نا لا زم آئے گا کیونکہ فعل عبد کا وجوب کبھی بندہ کے ارادہ واختیار سے ثابت ہوتا ہے اور کبھی بغیر ارادہ و اختیار کے وجوب ثابت ہوتا ہے جب بندہ کے ارادہ و اختیار کے بغیر وجوب ثابت ہو جیسے رعشہ کی حرکت وغیرہ تو اس میں کوئی شک نہیں کہ رعشہ کی حرکت اضطراری ہے اور وہ رعشہ والا مضطر ہے لیکن اگر وجوب ارادہ و اختیار مرج سے ثابت ہو تو اس سے اضطرار ثابت نہیں ہوتا گو کہ وجوب فعل ثابت ہو جاتا ہے تاہم یہ وجوب اختیار کے منافی نہیں بلکہ یہ وجوب تو اختیار کو محقق اور مؤکد کرتا ہے چنانچہ اختیار کی حرکت اور رعشہ کی حرکت کے درمیان فرق بدیہی ہے حالانکہ دونوں حرکتیں واجب ہیں مگر پہلی حرکت اختیار یہ ہے کہ ارادہ واختیار سے صادر ہوتی ہے اور دوسری حرکت
Then does it need some preponderator (murajjih) or not? If it needs a preponderator, then the division will return with the preponderator, such that this act with the preponderator is either necessary and obligatory, or permissible. On the first assumption it will be compulsory (idhtirari), and on the second assumption it will need another preponderator. Then speech continues about this last preponderator: is it obligatory or permissible? If obligatory then it is compulsory, and if permissible then it will need a third preponderator, thus infinite regress becomes necessary. And if it does not need a preponderator, such that the occurrence of the servant's act sometimes happens and sometimes does not happen, both states being equal without any renewal from the agent, then it is contingent (ittifaqi). His statement in the gloss: 'And it is not hidden from you that it is based on the negation of preference (awlawiyyah).' Objecting to the proof of the Mukhtasar, the noble Mirza Jan said in the gloss of Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'It is based on the negation of preference.' The summary of the objection: that the proof mentioned in the Mukhtasar is not sufficient for the act of the servant being compulsory, because this proof depends on the negation of preference. That is, in this proof only the obligatory act and the contingent act of the servant are mentioned, and the act that is preferable (awla), which is above permissible and below obligatory, is not mentioned. Therefore it is possible that the servant's act with a preponderator is preferential (awla) in its occurrence, and non-occurrence is permissible. In this case, the act occurs from the servant without obligation and contingency, and the servant is not compelled. Thus the mentioned proof of the Mukhtasar requires for its completeness regarding compulsion that preference also be negated, whereas the proof of the Mukhtasar does not negate it. Also, calling a possible act contingent is prohibited because possibility needs a favoring cause ('illah murajjihah), and when the act comes into existence due to the favoring cause, it cannot be contingent; or whatever needs a favoring cause is not contingent. In contrast, the author's method of argumentation adopted is not dependent on negation of preference, because when the preferable (awla) is not obligatory, the occurrence of the preferable is permissible, and in the presence of the preferable, the occurrence of what is not preferable is impossible because that would be giving preference to the preponderated (marjuh) and giving preference to the preponderated is impossible. Thus from this aspect, the author's proof is better than the proof of the Mukhtasar. Also, in terms of words and expressions, the proof of the Mukhtasar is long, as is apparent. Moreover, the word 'contingent' (ittifaqi) is excessive and inappropriate; from this perspective, the proof mentioned in Muslim is also shorter than that of the Mukhtasar. His statement in the gloss: 'There is something in it.' An objection was raised against one part of the Mukhtasar's proof that an act being contingent is possible, and the possible is not found without cause and reason, so at the time of the existence of the cause and reason, the act cannot be contingent. The author pointed to the answer to this mentioned objection with his statement 'fihi ma fihi'. The answer is: even if the contingent act is assumed to be with a causative preponderator, yet when it is not necessary of occurrence, but its existence and non-existence are both equal (permissible), indeed through this preponderating cause its existence is preferred, and it does not need another preponderator, then necessarily its being contingent follows, because by contingent is meant that which occurs without the intention [of the agent?] (marah? unclear). So then the agent will necessarily be non-choosing, because the contingent is non-choosing, since it occurs from the agent without his intent and will. The conclusion is that here by contingent is not meant that which is without any cause, such that the objection could be raised, but by contingent is meant that which is not by the agent's intent and will, even if it occurs from a cause and reason. Then on this interpretation no doubt remains that the contingent does not deserve praise and blame, nor reward and punishment. His statement: 'And the answer is that obligation by choice does not necessitate...' With this phrase the author (matn) answered the third proof of the Ash'ariyya. The summary of the answer: that we do not accept that due to the obligation of the servant's act, the act being compulsory and the servant being compelled necessarily follow, because the obligation of the servant's act is sometimes established by the servant's will and choice, and sometimes obligation is established without will and choice. When obligation is established without the servant's will and choice, such as the movement of tremor etc., then there is no doubt that the movement of tremor is compulsory and the person with tremor is compelled. But if the obligation is established by the preponderator of will and choice, then compulsion is not established, even if the obligation of the act is established. Nevertheless, this obligation does not contradict choice; rather, this obligation realizes and strengthens choice. Thus, the difference between a voluntary movement and a tremor movement is self-evident, even though both movements are obligatory (in the sense of occurring). But the first movement is voluntary because it occurs from will and choice, and the second movement...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of proofs concerning the nature of human actions, specifically addressing the question of whether actions are compulsory (idhtirari) or contingent (ittifaqi) and the implications for human agency. The author, in his commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, engages with criticisms from Mirza Jan on the proof presented in al-Mukhtasar and provides his own superior proof. He then responds to an Ash'ari argument that obligation (wujub) entails compulsion. The key point is the distinction between wujub bil-ikhtiyar (obligation through choice) and wujub bil-idhtirar (obligation without choice). The author argues that when an action is obligatory due to the agent's own will and choice, it does not negate choice but confirms it, as seen in the self-evident difference between a voluntary movement and an involuntary tremor. Thus, the Hanafi position upholds human responsibility while recognizing the necessity caused by external factors.

Key Terms
اتفاقی (ittifaqi): An action that occurs without the agent's intent and will, even if it has a cause; it does not necessarily mean without any cause.
اضطراری (idhtirari): An action that is necessary and compulsory, occurring without any choice from the agent, like an involuntary tremor.
وجوب بالاختیار (wujub bil-ikhtiyar): Obligation that arises from the agent's own choice and intention, e.g., a voluntary act that is required by Sharia.
اولی (awla): An action that is preferable but not obligatory; above permissible and below obligatory in the scale of legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes a voluntary movement from a tremor movement, even though both are 'wajib' (occur necessarily)?
A) The voluntary movement is from the agent's choice, while the tremor is without choice.
B) The voluntary movement is not wajib, while the tremor is wajib.
C) The voluntary movement is contingent (ittifaqi), while the tremor is not.
D) The tremor is from the agent's choice, while the voluntary movement is not.

Key Takeaway
Wujub bil-ikhtiyar does not imply compulsion; the Hanafi school preserves human responsibility by distinguishing the source of obligation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Necessity and Choice: Hanafi vs Ash'ari
Refuting Ash'ari claim that necessity negates choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi distinction between wujub with choice (ikhtiyar) and wujub without choice, and evaluate the Ash'ari counter-argument regarding causal determinism.

Core Idea
Necessity through your own will is not compulsion; it is choice itself.

How can a human action be both necessitated by a cause and truly voluntary? The Ash'ari school argues that if an action is caused (wajib), it becomes involuntary, stripping humans of real moral responsibility. In this passage, the Hanafi author responds by distinguishing two types of wujub: one that arises from external compulsion (like a tremor) and one that arises from the agent's own preponderating will and choice. The latter does not negate choice but rather affirms it. We will walk through this key argument and the ensuing debate.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari Claim
First, articulate the Ash'ari position: every event has a cause, and if a human action is necessitated by a cause (wujub), it is involuntary. Therefore, the agent is not truly choosing, and moral praise/blame is problematic.
The Ash'aris rely on the principle that causal necessity removes freedom.
2
Identify the Hanafi Distinction
The Hanafi author distinguishes two types of wujub: (1) wujub without the agent's will and choice (e.g., tremor), and (2) wujub established through the agent's own will and choice (ikhtiyar). Only the first is involuntary.
In the passage, the author uses the example of a tremor vs. a deliberate hand raise.
3
Explain Why Wujub with Ikhtiyār Does Not Negate Choice
When the cause of the action includes the agent's conception of the act, belief in its benefit, and firm resolve, the resulting wujub is actually the realization of choice. It does not make the agent compelled; rather, it verifies and strengthens choice.
The author says: 'this obligation verifies and strengthens choice'.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari Objection (Fadil Mirza Jan)
Fadil Mirza Jan objects that the will itself must have a cause to avoid infinite regress; if the will is caused, the agent is not free. The Hanafi response is that the Ash'ari reliance on an 'imaginary power' (qudrat mutawahhima) reduces the agent to a compelled figure in the guise of a chooser.
The author's marginal note rejects the Ash'ari sufficiency of formal choice (ikhtiyar suri).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The practical difference: In the Hanafi view, human actions are truly voluntary and thus subject to reward and punishment. In the Ash'ari view, humans 'acquire' actions without effective power, raising questions about the justice of divine judgment.
The author clearly favors the Hanafi position as the one that preserves moral accountability.

کیا گیا تھا کہ فعل اتفاقی ممکن ہے اور ممکن بغیر علت و سبب کے نہیں پایا جاتا ہے اس لئے وجود سبب و علت کے وقت فعل اتفاقی نہیں ہو سکتا۔ مصنف نے اپنے قول فیہ مافیہ سے اس منع مذکور کے جواب کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔ جواب : یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ فعل اتفاقی کو سبب مرج کے ساتھ فرض کیا جائے تا ہم جب وہ لازم الصدور نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اسکا وجود و عدم دونوں برابر ہوتا ہے ( جائز ہوتا ہے ) ہاں اس سبب مرجح کے سبب اس کے وجود کو تر جیح رہتی ہے اور کسی دوسرے مرجع کا بھی محتاج نہیں رہتا ہے تو ضرور اسکا اتفاقی ہونا لازم آئے گا کیونکہ اتفاقی سے وہ مراد ہے کہ مرح نام کے بغیر جو موجود ہو جائے پس اس وقت فاعل ضرور غیر مختار ہوگا کہ اتفاقی غیر مختار ہوتا ہے چونکہ فاعل سے اس کے قصد وارادہ کے بغیر صادر ہوتا ہے حاصل یہ ہے کہ یہاں اتفاقی سے یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ جو اصلاً بغیر سبب کے ہو یہاں تک کہ منع وارد کیا جائے بلکہ اتفاقی سے مراد یہ ہے کہ جو فاعل کے قصد وارادہ سے نہ ہو گر چہ وہ سبب وعلت سے صادر ہو پس اس معنی کی تقدیر پر کوئی شک نہیں رہ جاتا کہ اتفاقی مدح وذم اور ثواب و عقاب کا مستحق نہیں رہ جاتا۔
قوله والجواب أن الوجوب بالاختيار لايوجب الخ
اس عبارت سے ماتن نے اشاعرہ کی دلیل ثالث کا جواب دیا ہے۔
جواب : - کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ فعل عبد کے وجوب کے سبب سے فعل کا اضطراری ہونا اور بندہ کا منظر ہو نا لا زم آئے گا کیونکہ فعل عبد کا وجوب کبھی بندہ کے ارادہ واختیار سے ثابت ہوتا ہے اور کبھی بغیر ارادہ و اختیار کے وجوب ثابت ہوتا ہے جب بندہ کے ارادہ و اختیار کے بغیر وجوب ثابت ہو جیسے رعشہ کی حرکت وغیرہ تو اس میں کوئی شک نہیں کہ رعشہ کی حرکت اضطراری ہے اور وہ رعشہ والا مضطر ہے لیکن اگر وجوب ارادہ و اختیار مرج سے ثابت ہو تو اس سے اضطرار ثابت نہیں ہوتا گو کہ وجوب فعل ثابت ہو جاتا ہے تاہم یہ وجوب اختیار کے منافی نہیں بلکہ یہ وجوب تو اختیار کو محقق اور مؤکد کرتا ہے چنانچہ اختیار کی حرکت اور رعشہ کی حرکت کے درمیان فرق بدیہی ہے حالانکہ دونوں حرکتیں واجب ہیں مگر پہلی حرکت اختیار یہ ہے کہ ارادہ واختیار سے صادر ہوتی ہے اور دوسری حرکت اختیار یہ نہیں بلکہ اضطرار یہ ہے وجہ فرق ظاہر ہے کہ پہلی میں قدرت و اختیار دخیل ہے اور دوسری میں ایسا نہیں ۔ چنانچہ فعل اختیاری کہتے ہی ہیں اس فعل کو جس کا فاعل تصور کرنے کے بعد، اس کے فائدہ کی تصدیق یا کم از کم تخیل حاصل ہونے کے بعد اپنے عزم مصم سے اس فعل کے کرنے کا قصد کرے۔ غرض یہ کہ جو فعل ان امور کے بعد یعنی ذہن فاعل میں اس فعل کی تصویر، اس کے فائدہ کی تصدیق یا تخیل اور فاعل کا قصد و ارادہ اور عزم مصمم کے بعد موجود ہو اختیاری کہلاتا ہے گو کہ یہ فعل اپنی علت کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے واجب ہو جاتا ہے تاہم یہ وجوب اس فعل کو حیز اختیار (دائرہ اختیار ) سے خارج نہیں کرتا اس جگہ ماتن نے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے۔
قدید فع بانه قد تقرر أن الاراده ليست اعتبارية محضة فلها مؤثر آخر وليس اراده اخرى ضرورة فذالك المؤثر موجب فيجب الفعل ،اتفاقا اقول اتمامه على الاشاعرة ممنوع فانهم اكتفوا بوجود قدرة متوهمة ومن ههنا قالوا أن وجود الاختيارى الصورى كاف فى التكليف وان العبد مجبور في صورة مختار فافهم حاشیہ مذکورہ میں ماتن نے فاضل مرزا جان کے اس جواب کا ذکر کیا ہے جو فاضل صاحب نے اشاعرہ کی حمایت میں مصنف کے جواب کو رد کرتے ہوئے دیا ہے اور پھر ماتن نے فاضل کے جواب کا جواب دیا ہے ملاحظہ ہو۔
جواب فاضل : شرح مختصر کے حاشیہ میں فاضل مرزا جان نے کہا ہے کہ اگر بندہ سے کوئی فعل ارادہ سے صادر ہوا تو اس ارادہ کے لئے کسی دوسرے ارادہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی کہ وہ ارادہ بھی کسی دوسرے ارادہ سے اور دوسرا ارادہ کسی اور ارادہ سے صادر ہو ورنہ مبدا میں تسلسل لازم آئے گا، پھر فعل کے صدور کے وقت ہم اپنے نفس میں ارادہ واحدہ ہی پاتے ہیں چنانچہ یہاں کوئی ، دوسرا ارادہ نہیں پایا جاتا جو ارادہ اولی سے متعلق ہو پھر ہم یہ بھی بالبداہتہ جانتے ہیں کہ ارادہ ایسا کوئی اعتبار یہ محضہ شئی نہیں کہ اس میں حاجت تاثیر نہیں بلکہ اس کے لئے کوئی مؤثر آخر یا علت موجب ضرور چاہئے پس اسوقت لازم ہے کہ علت ارادہ ، ارادہ مرید کا غیر ہوگی اب وہ غیر ( علت ارادہ) ضروری طور پر یا تو اس امر کی طرف منتھی ہوگی جو بندہ سے بالا یجاب صادر ہو یا اس امر کی طرف منسوب ہو جو اللہ تعالیٰ سے صادر ہو،
It was said that coincidental action is possible, and possible is not found without cause and reason, therefore at the time of existence of cause and reason, the action cannot be coincidental. The author pointed to the answer to this mentioned objection with his words 'fihi ma fihi'. The answer: Even if the coincidental action is assumed with a preponderating cause, yet when it is not necessarily occurring but its existence and non-existence are equal (permissible), yes, through this preponderating cause its existence gains preponderance and it does not need another preponderator, then necessarily its being coincidental will be necessary, because by coincidental what is meant is that which occurs without a preponderator. Then at that time the doer will necessarily be non-choosing, because coincidental is non-choosing, in that it issues from the doer without his intent and will. The gist is that here by coincidental is not meant that which is without cause altogether such that the objection could be raised, rather by coincidental is meant that which is not from the doer's intent and will, even if it issues from a cause. Upon this estimation, no doubt remains that the coincidental is not deserving of praise and blame, reward and punishment. 
His statement: 'And the answer is that obligation with choice does not necessitate...' With this expression the author answered the third proof of the Ash'aris. The gist of the answer: We do not concede that due to the obligation of the slave's action, the action's being compelled and the slave being compelled necessarily follows, because the obligation of the slave's action is sometimes established by the slave's will and choice, and sometimes obligation is established without will and choice. When obligation is established without the slave's will and choice, like the movement of a tremor etc., then there is no doubt that the tremor's movement is compelled and the person with tremor is compelled. But if obligation is established by the preponderating will and choice, then compulsion is not established even though the obligation of the action is established; nevertheless, this obligation is not contrary to choice, rather this obligation verifies and strengthens choice. Thus the difference between voluntary movement and tremor movement is self-evident, even though both movements are obligatory. But the first movement is voluntary because it issues from will and choice, and the second movement is not voluntary but compelled. The reason for the difference is clear: in the first, power and choice are involved; in the second, not. Thus voluntary action is called that action after the doer forms a conception of it, after confirming or at least imagining its benefit, he intends to do it with firm resolve. In short, the action that occurs after these matters – i.e., the image of the action in the doer's mind, confirmation or imagination of its benefit, and the doer's intent and will and firm resolve – is called voluntary, even if this action becomes necessary when attributed to its cause. Nevertheless, this obligation does not remove the action from the sphere of choice. Here the author said in the margin: 'It has been established that will is not a mere mental consideration; rather it has another effect, and there is no other will necessarily. So that effect necessitates, so the action becomes necessary, unanimously. I say: its completion by the Ash'aris is rejected, for they sufficed with the existence of an imaginary power, and from here they said that the existence of formal choice suffices for obligation, and that the slave is compelled in the form of a chooser. Understand.' In this mentioned marginal note, the author mentioned the answer of Fadil Mirza Jan, which he gave in support of the Ash'aris, refuting the author's answer, and then the author answered Fadil's answer. Consider. 
Fadil's answer: In the margin of Sharh Mukhtasar, Fadil Mirza Jan said that if an action issues from the slave with will, then that will does not need another will, such that that will issues from another will, and the second from a third, otherwise infinite regress would be necessary. Then at the time of the action's occurrence, we find in ourselves only a single will. Thus here no other will is found that pertains to the first will. Then we also know by self-evidence that will is not a mere mental consideration such that it has no need of efficacy; rather it necessarily requires another effect or necessitating cause. So then it is necessary that the cause of the will is other than the will of the willing person. Now that other (cause of will) will necessarily terminate either at something that issues from the slave necessarily or at something that issues from Allah.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between Hanafi and Ash'ari schools over human action and causal necessity. The Ash'aris argue that if an action is necessitated by a cause (wujub), it becomes involuntary (idtirari), negating human choice. The Hanafi author responds by distinguishing two types of obligation (wujub): (1) wujub without the agent's will and choice, like a tremor, which is truly compelled; and (2) wujub through the agent's will and preponderating choice (ikhtiyar). In the latter, the action is necessary in terms of causal connection (since the cause includes the will), but the agent is still choosing because his will and intent are part of the cause. The author emphasizes that a voluntary action requires conception of the act, belief in its benefit, and firm resolve; even if this chain is causally determined, it does not negate the experience of choice. The 'hashiya' mentions Mirza Jan's Ash'ari-leaning objection that will itself must have a cause to avoid infinite regress, leading to divine determinism. The author counters that the Ash'ari position reduces human power to a mere formality, making the slave compelled in the guise of a chooser. The passage thus upholds the Hanafi position that actions issued with genuine will and power are voluntary and subject to moral responsibility, even if they are ultimately caused by Allah's creation of power and choice in the slave.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub): Necessity or obligation of an action's occurrence due to a cause; can be voluntary or involuntary.
اتفاقی (ittifaqi): Coincidental: occurring without the agent's intent, even if caused; not subject to moral evaluation.
اختیار (ikhtiyar): Choice: the agent's deliberate will based on conception and benefit assessment, leading to firm resolve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, why does the obligation (wujub) of a voluntary action not make it involuntary?
A) Because voluntary actions have no cause.
B) Because the agent's own will and choice are part of the cause of the action.
C) Because the agent can always choose otherwise despite the cause.
D) Because obligation only applies to involuntary actions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that causal necessity (wujub) does not negate genuine human choice when the necessity arises from the agent's own will and intention.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
The Hanafi-Ash'ari Debate: Are Human Actions Truly Voluntary or Compelled?
Responding to Ash'ari claim that human actions are compelled but appear voluntary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari argument that human actions are compelled and state the Hanafi response based on the definition of ikhtiyari.

Core Idea
Voluntary action requires genuine power and choice; compulsion negates moral responsibility.

This passage delves into a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafi/Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding the nature of human action. The Ash'ari argument, as presented by Fadhil Mirza Jan, claims that since the will itself is caused by an external force, all human acts are ultimately compelled, albeit with a formal voluntary appearance. The Hanafi response, articulated in the marginal note, insists that voluntary acts are defined by the agent's conceptualization, benefit assessment, and firm resolve, and that even if necessitated by a cause, they remain within the sphere of choice. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping how each school grounds moral responsibility and legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of voluntary action (ikhtiyari)
According to the passage, identify the components that make an action voluntary: conceptualization of the act, verification or imagination of its benefit, and firm resolve (intention).
The passage explicitly states: 'the image of the act in the agent's mind, verification or imagination of its benefit, and the agent's intention, will, and firm resolve.'
2
Identify the Ash'ari argument
Summarize Mirza Jan's argument that will requires an external cause, leading to the will being necessary and thus the act compelled.
He argues: will has an effect, no second will, cause external, therefore will is necessitated, act becomes necessary.
3
Identify the Hanafi response
How does the author rebut the Ash'ari claim? Focus on the concept of 'necessity by choice' (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) and the observation that the Ash'ari view leads to denial of rational good/evil and limits Allah's freedom.
The Hanafi position: the act remains voluntary despite being necessitated by its cause; the Ash'ari position is countered by its own acknowledged objections.
4
Examine the rational proof (prerequisites argument)
Explain the second argument: if all prerequisites for an act are present, the act must occur, otherwise preference without a preponderant would occur. This is used to support the claim of compulsion.
The author presents this as an alternative formulation of the Ash'ari case.
5
Evaluate the acknowledged objections
List the three objections Mirza Jan admits: 1) no rational good/evil, 2) Allah not absolutely free, 3) aligns with Mu'tazilite view of God as necessitating agent. Discuss their impact on the Ash'ari position.
These objections show the theological costs of the Ash'ari argument.

اختیار یہ نہیں بلکہ اضطرار یہ ہے وجہ فرق ظاہر ہے کہ پہلی میں قدرت و اختیار دخیل ہے اور دوسری میں ایسا نہیں ۔ چنانچہ فعل اختیاری کہتے ہی ہیں اس فعل کو جس کا فاعل تصور کرنے کے بعد، اس کے فائدہ کی تصدیق یا کم از کم تخیل حاصل ہونے کے بعد اپنے عزم مصم سے اس فعل کے کرنے کا قصد کرے۔ غرض یہ کہ جو فعل ان امور کے بعد یعنی ذہن فاعل میں اس فعل کی تصویر، اس کے فائدہ کی تصدیق یا تخیل اور فاعل کا قصد و ارادہ اور عزم مصمم کے بعد موجود ہو اختیاری کہلاتا ہے گو کہ یہ فعل اپنی علت کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے واجب ہو جاتا ہے تاہم یہ وجوب اس فعل کو حیز اختیار (دائرہ اختیار ) سے خارج نہیں کرتا اس جگہ ماتن نے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے۔

قدید فع بانه قد تقرر أن الاراده ليست اعتبارية محضة فلها مؤثر
آخر وليس اراده اخرى ضرورة فذالك المؤثر موجب فيجب الفعل ،اتفاقا اقول اتمامه على الاشاعرة ممنوع فانهم اكتفوا بوجود قدرة متوهمة ومن ههنا قالوا أن
وجود الاختيارى الصورى كاف فى التكليف وان العبد مجبور في صورة مختار فافهم حاشیہ مذکورہ میں ماتن نے فاضل مرزا جان کے اس جواب کا ذکر کیا ہے جو فاضل صاحب نے اشاعرہ کی حمایت میں مصنف کے جواب کو رد کرتے ہوئے دیا ہے اور پھر ماتن نے فاضل کے جواب کا جواب دیا ہے ملاحظہ ہو۔

جواب فاضل : شرح مختصر کے حاشیہ میں فاضل مرزا جان نے کہا ہے کہ اگر بندہ سے کوئی فعل ارادہ سے صادر ہوا تو اس ارادہ کے لئے کسی دوسرے ارادہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی کہ وہ ارادہ بھی کسی دوسرے ارادہ سے اور دوسرا ارادہ کسی اور ارادہ سے صادر ہو ورنہ مبدا میں تسلسل لازم آئے گا، پھر فعل کے صدور کے وقت ہم اپنے نفس میں ارادہ واحدہ ہی پاتے ہیں چنانچہ یہاں کوئی ، دوسرا ارادہ نہیں پایا جاتا جو ارادہ اولی سے متعلق ہو پھر ہم یہ بھی بالبداہتہ جانتے ہیں کہ ارادہ ایسا کوئی اعتبار یہ محضہ شئی نہیں کہ اس میں حاجت تاثیر نہیں بلکہ اس کے لئے کوئی مؤثر آخر یا علت موجب ضرور چاہئے پس اسوقت لازم ہے کہ علت ارادہ ، ارادہ مرید کا غیر ہوگی اب وہ غیر ( علت ارادہ) ضروری طور پر یا تو اس امر کی طرف منتھی ہوگی جو بندہ سے بالا یجاب صادر ہو یا اس امر کی طرف منسوب ہو جو اللہ تعالیٰ سے صادر ہو،

بہر دو صورت جب تک یہ امر محقق نہیں ہوگا ارادہ تحقیق نہ ہوگا اور جب ارادہ ہی متحقق نہ ہوگا تو فعل کا صدور نہیں ہو گا۔ البتہ جب اس امر کا صدور ہوگا تو ارادہ واجب ہوگا اور جب ارادہ واجب ہوا تو فعل کا صدور لازم ویقینی ہوگا اب بندہ سے صدور فعل واجب ہوگا پس اس وقت بندہ سے صدور فعل کا وجوب اس امر کے سبب سے ہوا جس کے وجود میں بندہ کو اختیار نہیں ہے اس کا نام اضطرار ہے رہے ارادہ کی مداخلت تو اس اضطرار کو مضر نہیں ۔ بلکہ وہ ارادہ تو اضطرار کو اور پکا کر رہا ہے کیونکہ یہ ارادہ تو خود واجبہ اضطرار یہ ہے لہذا اس سے جو صادر ہوگا وہ ضرور اضطراری ہوگا چونکہ اختیاری وہ ہے جس کا فعل و ترک دونوں جائز ہو اور یہ ایسا فعل ہے جو ارادہ واجبہ کے سبب سے ایسا واجب ہو گیا کہ اس کا ترک ممکن نہیں اور جس کا ترک ممکن نہیں وہ اضطراری ہوتا ہے اور اضطراری میں حسن و قبح نہیں ہوتا۔

دوسری تقریر فاضل مذکور کی دلیل کی تقریر اس انداز میں بھی کر سکتے ہیں۔ کہ تمام وہ شرائط ولوازم جن پر وجود فعل موقوف ہے وہ جملہ موقوف علیہ تحقیق ہیں یا نہیں، بر تقدیر ثانی یعنی فعل کے جملہ موقوف علیہ کا حق نہ ہوا تو ظاہر ہے کہ فعل موجود نہ ہوگا اور بر تقدیر اول یعنی اگر فعل کے تمام موقوف علیہ کا تحقق ہوا تو فعل کا وجود لازم ہوگا اور اضطرار لازم آئے گا کیونکہ اس وقت اس کا ترک جائز نہ ہوگا ورنہ (اگر فعل کے جمیع موقوف علیہ پائے گئے اور وجود فعل لازم نہ ہوا) ترجیح بلا مرج یا خلاف مفروض لازم آئے گا اور یہ باطل اس لئے ماننا پڑے گا کہ جملہ موقوف علیہ پائے جانے کی صورت میں فعل کا وجود لازماً واضطرارا ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا مصنف کا جواب اشاعرہ کے رد میں غیر مفید ہے اس لئے کہ ان دونوں دلیلوں میں وجوب بالاختیار اضطرار کو لازم کر رہا ہے۔

اعتراف : ۔ تاہم جناب فاضل مذکور نے یہ اعتراف کیا ہے کہ یہاں تین اشکالات بہر حال وارد ہوں گے۔
(۱) افعال عباد اور افعال باری تعالٰی میں حسن و قبح عقلی نہیں رہ جائے گا۔
(۲) باری تعالیٰ اپنے افعال میں مختار مطلق نہیں رہ جائے گا بلکہ وہ ایسا مختار ہوگا جو شائبہ
ایجاب سے متصف ہوگا جیسا کہ بعض معتزلہ اور فلاسفہ کا مختار ہے کہ باری تعالیٰ فاعل موجب ہے اور
جمہور متکلمین اس کے خلاف گئے ہیں۔
Volition is not this, rather compulsion is this. The reason for the difference is clear: in the first, power and choice are involved, but in the second, not so. Thus, a voluntary act is called that act which, after the agent conceives it, after verifying its benefit or at least imagining it, intends to do it with firm resolve. In short, an act that occurs after these matters—i.e., the image of the act in the agent's mind, verification or imagination of its benefit, and the agent's intention, will, and firm resolve—is called voluntary. Even if this act becomes necessary in relation to its cause, this necessity does not remove the act from the sphere of choice. At this point, the author (matn) said in a marginal note:

'It has already been established that will is not a mere mental consideration; it has another effect, and there is no other will necessarily. That effect is necessitating, so the act becomes necessary, agreed. I say: its completion is denied against the Ash'aris, for they are satisfied with the existence of an imaginary power. From this they said that the existence of a formal voluntary act is sufficient for obligation, and that the servant is compelled in the form of a free agent. Understand.'

In the aforementioned marginal note, the author mentioned this response of Fadhil Mirza Jan, which the Fadhil gave in support of the Ash'aris, rebutting the author's response, and then the author responded to the Fadhil's response. Observe.

Fadhil's response: In the marginal note of Sharh Mukhtasar, Fadhil Mirza Jan said: If an act proceeds from a servant by will, then there is no need for another will for that will, such that that will proceeds from another will, and the second from another, otherwise infinite regress would be necessary. Then, at the time of the act's occurrence, we find only a single will in ourselves; thus, no second will is found here that relates to the first will. Then we also know by direct intuition that will is not such a mere mental thing that it has no need for effect; rather, it must have another effect or a necessitating cause. So then it is necessary that the cause of the will is other than the willer's will. Now that other (cause of will) will necessarily either terminate in something that proceeds by compulsion from the servant, or be attributed to something that proceeds from Allah. In either case, as long as this matter is not realized, the will will not be realized, and when the will is not realized, the act will not occur. However, when this matter occurs, the will becomes necessary, and when the will becomes necessary, the act's occurrence becomes necessary and certain. Now the act's occurrence from the servant becomes necessary. So at that time, the necessity of the act's occurrence from the servant is due to a matter in whose existence the servant has no choice; this is called compulsion. As for the intervention of will, it does not harm this compulsion; rather, that will only strengthens the compulsion, because this will itself is necessarily compelled. Therefore, whatever proceeds from it will necessarily be compelled. Since a voluntary act is that of which both doing and leaving are permissible, and this act is such that due to the necessary will it became so necessary that its leaving is not possible, and that which leaving is not possible is compelled, and there is no good or evil in the compelled.

Second presentation: The argument of the mentioned Fadhil can also be presented in this manner: All those conditions and requisites upon which the existence of the act depends—are they all actualized or not? In the second case (i.e., if not all prerequisites are actualized), obviously the act will not exist. In the first case (i.e., if all prerequisites are actualized), the existence of the act becomes necessary and compulsion becomes necessary, because at that time its leaving is not permissible. Otherwise (if all prerequisites are present and the act's existence is not necessary), preference without a preponderant or contradiction of the premise would be necessary, and this is false because when all prerequisites are present, the act's existence necessarily and compellingly occurs. Therefore, the author's response in rebutting the Ash'aris is useless, because in both arguments, the obligatory by choice implies compulsion.

Acknowledgment: Nevertheless, the mentioned Fadhil acknowledged that three objections will inevitably arise here:
(1) There will no longer remain rational good and evil in the actions of servants and the actions of Allah.
(2) Allah will no longer be absolutely free in His actions; rather, He will be a free agent tainted with necessity, like the free agent of some Mu'tazilites and philosophers who say that Allah is a necessitating agent, contrary to what the majority of theologians hold.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly dispute within the genre of usul al-fiqh, addressing the theological underpinnings of human action and moral responsibility—a debate between the Hanafi/Maturidi position and the Ash'ari school. The author of Al-Ashraf al-Nu'ut (the super-commentary) is engaging critically with the marginal note of Fadhil Mirza Jan, who defended the Ash'ari view. The passage begins by defining voluntary action (fi'l ikhtiyari) as an act that proceeds after conceptualization, benefit assessment (or imagination), and firm resolve. Even if the act becomes necessary due to its cause, this necessity does not negate its voluntary nature according to the Hanafi view. The author then quotes a marginal note stating that will is not a mere mental construct; it requires an external effect. Ash'aris, however, are content with an imaginary power and claim that formal voluntary action suffices for obligation, asserting that the servant is compelled in the form of a free agent. Mirza Jan's response argues that a single will is found without infinite regress, and this will must have a cause external to the willer. That cause either comes from the servant by compulsion or from Allah. Either way, the will becomes necessary, making the act compelled. Thus, the existence of will actually reinforces compulsion. The author then presents a second rational argument: if all prerequisites for an act are present, the act must necessarily occur, otherwise it would involve preference without a preponderant. Hence, the author's attempt to refute the Ash'aris by distinguishing voluntary from compelled is ineffective. Finally, Mirza Jan acknowledges three objections: (1) rational good and evil disappear, (2) Allah's absolute freedom is compromised, aligning with the Mu'tazilite view of a necessitating agent. This passage illustrates the deep engagement with Kalam within Hanafi usul, showing how theological commitments shape legal theory.

Key Terms
اختیاری (ikhtiyari): An action that proceeds after the agent's conceptualization, verification of benefit (or imagination), and firm resolve; doing and leaving are both possible.
اضطرار (idtirar): Necessity or compulsion where the act's omission is impossible; no moral evaluation applies.
ارادہ (irada): Will or intention; a quality that has a real effect and requires an external cause.
وجوب بالاختیار (wujub bil-ikhtiyar): Necessity by choice; an act becomes necessary due to a cause but remains within the sphere of choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key characteristic of a voluntary action (ikhtiyari) that distinguishes it from a compelled action (idtirari)?
A) It is performed without any intention.
B) Both doing and leaving are possible.
C) It is caused by an external force without the agent's knowledge.
D) It always results in reward or punishment.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are truly voluntary when they proceed from conceptualization, benefit assessment, and firm resolve; compulsion would negate moral responsibility.

Two Rational Arguments Refuting Divine Compulsion
Two rational arguments refuting the claim that God's actions are necessitated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaws (tasalsul and compulsion) in the opponent's argument that possible actions must become obligatory, and explain the Hanafi position that maintains divine freedom and human capacity.

Core Idea
If every divine will requires another will, an infinite regress destroys the argument — Allah's power is not bound by necessity.

This passage presents two rational arguments refuting a position that would conclude Allah's actions are compelled. The opponent claims that any possible action must become obligatory before existing, leading to necessity in the divine will. The Hanafi commentator shows that this leads either to an infinite regress of wills or to the conclusion that Allah is compelled—both impossible. This is a classic usul dispute about divine attributes and human free will, defending both Allah's absolute power and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Opponent's Position
The opponent argues that a possible action cannot exist unless it becomes obligatory (wajib). The commentator states this as 'the possible, as long as it does not become obligatory, cannot exist.' Identify the premise and the conclusion they draw about divine actions.
This is the starting point for both refutations.
2
First Refutation: Infinite Regress of Wills
If the obligation arises from a divine will, and that will itself requires another will to become obligatory, then we have an infinite chain (tasalsul). Since infinite regress is impossible, the opponent's reasoning fails. Trace the logic: will→will→will... ad infinitum.
Tasalsul is a standard argument in Hanafi usul against necessitarianism.
3
Second Refutation: Compulsion from Necessity
If the will is not from another will but from a prior command that is necessitated, then once that command is actualized, the will and action become obligatory. This makes Allah's actions compelled (mudtar), which contradicts His absolute power and freedom. The commentator says 'the obligation of the action is compulsion'.
This shows that the opponent's view leads to a theologically unacceptable conclusion.
4
Turning the Tables: The Parity Argument
The commentator then argues: if the opponent claims that Allah's will is in the realm of choice (ikhtiyar), then the same reasoning applies to human actions. The opponent's answer for divine actions becomes the Hanafi answer for human actions, preserving human free will without compulsion.
This is a powerful refutation by analogy (qiyas).
5
Conclusion: Divine Freedom and Human Capacity
The Hanafi position affirms that Allah is absolutely powerful and free (qadir mutlaq mukhtar haqiqi), and that human actions are not compelled. The refutation shows that the opponent's rational argument fails on its own terms.
This lesson is a model of aqli istidlal in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text

نہیں ہوسکتا اور مرجوح کی ترجیح محال ہے لہذا اس کی نقیض وجود جو رائج ہے واجب ہوگا پس فعل باری تعالیٰ واجب ہو گیا اور وجوب موجب اضطرار ہے تو فعل باری تعالیٰ اضطراری اور باری تعالی معاذ اللہ مضطر ٹھہرا حالانکہ وہ قادر مطلق مختار حقیقی ہے۔
تقریر دوم :- بلفظ دیگر جواب کی تقریر اس طرح بھی کی جاتی ہے۔
کر فعل باری تعالیٰ ممکن ہے اور ممکن جب تک واجب نہ ہوگا موجود نہیں ہو سکتا اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ فعل کا صدور یا وجوب فعل ارادہ الہی سے ہوگا پس ہم ارادہ الہی پر کلام کرتے ہیں کہ وہ ارادہ کسی دوسرے ارادہ سے صادر ہوا اور دوسرا ارادہ تیسرے ارادہ سے حاصل ہوا ھکذ ا الى غیر النھایہ، تو تسلسل لازم آئے گا اور اگر وہ ارادہ کسی دوسرے ارادہ سے صادر نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ اس امر کی طرف منتہی ہے جو باری تعالیٰ سے بالا یجاب صادر ہوا نے ، تو جب تک وہ امر متحقق نہ ہوگا ارادہ نہ ہوگا اور جب ارادہ نہ ہوگا تو فعل موجود نہ ہوگا اور جب وہ امر سنتھی الیہ تحقیق ہوا تو ارادہ پایا گیا اور وجوب ارادہ سے، وجوب فعل وہ امر متحقق ہوگا کیونکہ معلول کا خلف علت تامہ سے ممتنع ہے اور شک نہیں کہ وجوب فعل ہی اضطرار ہے لہذا افعال باری تعالیٰ کا اضطرار یہ ہونا لازم آیا۔ اور اگر وہ ارادہ صورت اختیار میں ہے تو افعال باری تعالیٰ میں جو تمہارا جواب ہو گا وھی افعال عباد میں ہمارا جواب ہوگا!
Cannot happen, and preferring the less preponderant is impossible; therefore its opposite, which is existence, being prevalent, becomes obligatory. Thus the action of Allah Most High becomes obligatory, and obligation necessitates compulsion, so the action of Allah Most High is compelled and Allah – God forbid – becomes compelled, while He is absolutely powerful, truly free.
Second presentation: In other words, the answer is also presented like this: The action of Allah Most High is possible, and the possible, as long as it does not become obligatory, cannot exist. And it is apparent that the occurrence of the action or the obligation of the action will be from the divine will. So we discuss the divine will: whether that will came from another will, and a second will came from a third will, and so on ad infinitum, so infinite regress would be necessary. And if that will did not originate from another will but rather it terminates at a command that came from Allah Most High without necessity, then as long as that command is not actualized, there will be no will, and when there is no will, the action will not exist. And when that terminating command is actualized, the will is found, and from the obligation of the will, the obligation of the action, that command will be actualized because the effect cannot be contrary to the complete cause. And there is no doubt that the obligation of the action is compulsion; thus the actions of Allah Most High being compelled becomes necessary. And if that will is in the realm of choice, then your answer regarding the actions of Allah Most High will be our answer regarding the actions of servants!
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents two rational arguments (تقریر اول و دوم) refuting an opponent's position that would lead to the conclusion that Allah's actions are compelled (مضطر). The opponent likely argued that if a possible action is to exist, it must become obligatory (واجب) because otherwise it could not come into existence. The commentator refutes this by showing that it leads to a vicious regress (تسلسل) if every will requires another will, or to the conclusion that Allah's actions are compulsory if the will is necessitated. The second argument turns the tables: if the opponent claims that Allah's will is in the realm of choice (اختیار), then the same reasoning can be applied to human actions, preserving human free will. This is a classic Hanafi defense of human capacity (قدرة) and divine power (قدرة الله) without falling into either compulsion (جبر) or delegation (تفویض).

Key Terms
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress of wills, argued as impossible.
اضطرار (idtirār): Compulsion; the state of being forced, here applied to divine actions as a consequence of the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the commentator identify in the opponent's argument that a possible action must become obligatory to exist?
A) Infinite regress of wills
B) Contradiction in the concept of possibility
C) Denial of divine omnipotence
D) Violation of causality

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi refutation of divine compulsion rests on the impossibility of infinite regress and the preservation of divine choice, which symmetrically applies to human actions.

Schools on Human Capacity: From Determinism to Kasb
Matn and Urdu commentary listing four theological positions on human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four theological positions on human power (qudra) and explain the Hanafi doctrine of kasb.

Core Idea
The Hanafi kasb resolves the tension between divine omnipotence and human accountability by assigning human power to influence intention, not creation.

This passage from Musallam al-Thubut surveys four schools on human capacity: Jahmiyya (pure determinism), Mu'tazila (human self-efficacy), Ash'ariyya (imagined power), and Hanafiyya (kasb). Understanding these positions is crucial for grasping the Hanafi theory of action and legal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four schools
List the Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, and Hanafiyya as presented in the text.
2
Analyze each school's view on human power
For each, state whether power exists, its nature, and its role in action.
3
Locate the author's critique
Note how the author dismisses the first three positions as sophistry, magian dualism, or disguised determinism.
4
Define the Hanafi kasb
Extract the precise definition: directing created power towards a determined intention; God creates the act by custom.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Explain the practical impact: only kasb makes taklif meaningful; the other views either negate responsibility or compromise tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : فائدة عند الجهمية الذين هم الجبرية حقا لا قدرة للعبد بل هو كالجماد، وهذا اسفسطة وعند المعتزلة له قدرة مؤثرة في افعاله وهم مجوس هذه الامة ومافهموا ان الامكان ليس من شانه افادة الوجود وعند اهل الحق له قدرة كاسبة لكن عند الاشعرية ليس معنى ذالك الاوجود قدرة متوهمة مع الفعل بلا مدخلية اصلا قالوا ذالك كاف فى التكليف والحق انه كفو للجبر وعندالحنفية الكسب صرف القدرة المخلوقة الى القصد المصمم الى الفعل فلهاتا ثير في القصد المذكور ويخلق الله سبحانه الفعل المقصود عند ذالك بالعادة

Text of al-Musallam: A benefit: According to the Jahmiyya, who are truly the Jabriyya, the servant has no power at all; rather he is like an inanimate object. This is sophistry. According to the Mu'tazila, he has an effective power in his actions, and they are the Magians of this ummah. They did not understand that possibility is not of a nature to give existence. According to the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq), he has an acquisitive power (qudra kasiba). But according to the Ash'ariyya, the meaning of that is not except the existence of an imagined power with the act without any intervention at all. They said that is sufficient for obligation. The truth is that it is similar to Jabr (determinism). According to the Hanafiyya, Kasb is directing the created power towards a determined intention for the act. So it has an effect on that intention, and Allah Most High creates the intended act at that time by way of custom.
Translation: According to the Jahmiyya sect, who are in reality the Jabriyya, the servant has absolutely no power; rather he is like an inanimate object (pure compelled), and this is extremely wrong. According to the Mu'tazila, the servant has such an effective power that it influences his actions, and they are the Magians of this ummah, and they did not understand that possibility is not characterized by giving existence to anything. According to Ahl al-Haqq, the servant has a power of acquisition. However, according to the Ash'ariyya, the meaning of the existence of power is the existence of an imagined power with the act without any intervention from the servant. And they say that this is sufficient for being obligated. The truth is that this statement resembles Jabr. According to the Hanafiyya, Kasb is the turning of the created power towards the determined intention for the act, so this power has an effect on that intention, and at that time Allah Most High, by way of custom, creates the act.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a matn (from Musallam al-Thubut) presenting the views of four schools on the issue of human capacity (قدرة العبد). The author first introduces the Jahmiyya who completely deny human power, calling their view sophistry. Then the Mu'tazila who claim humans have an effective power, which the author criticizes as resembling magian dualism. Then the Ash'ariyya who posit an 'imagined power' with no real effect, which the author deems similar to Jabr. Finally, the Hanafiyya position of kasb: human power is created by God but the servant 'acquires' the act by directing this power towards a firm intention (القصد المصمم), and God creates the act concomitantly by his custom (عادة). This preserves both divine omnipotence and human responsibility. The Hanafi view is presented as the correct one.

Key Terms
کسب (kasb): Hanafi doctrine: directing created power towards a determined intention, with God creating the act by custom.
قدرت مؤثره (qudrat mu'aththira): Effective power claimed by Mu'tazila to create actions independently.
قدرت کاسبہ (qudrat kāsiba): Acquisitive power attributed to Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'ari and Hanafi) but with different meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the role of human power (qudra) in action?
A) It creates the act independently
B) It influences the intention, then God creates the act
C) It is merely an imagined power with no effect
D) It is completely absent like in Jabriyya

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi doctrine of kasb distinguishes itself by holding that human power influences intention, not the act itself, preserving both divine omnipotence and human responsibility.
